This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which visited
Mexico in October 1984 and a post-appraisal mission in February 1985. The
appraisal mission comprised Messrs. J. Gutman (economist), M. Lal (railway
engineer), Z. Raanan (financial analyst), F. Chapman (consultant financial
analyst), J. Kesson (consultant engineer) and (Ms.) M. Pokorny (consultant
economist). The report has been edited by Miss V. Foster.

MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
I. PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:       Banco Nacional de Obras y Servicios Publicos, S.A. (BANOBRAS)
Guarantor:      United Mexican States
Project
Executing
Agency:         Secretaria de Comunicaciones y Transportes (SCT)
Ferrocarriles Nacionales de Mexico (N de M)
Amount:         US$300 million
Terms:          Repayment in 15 years, including three years of grace, at the
standard variable interest rate.
Relending
Terms:          BANOBRAS would relend the proceeds of the loan to N de M and
the Government (in the case of SCT) in US dollars oTI the same
terms and conditions as the Bank loan. N de M and the
Government would assume the foreign exchange risk for their
respective shares of the proposed loan.
Project
Description:   The proposed project provides for investment and operational
and financial improvements to the Mexican railway system
during the period 1985-1988. Investment items supported by
the loan cover both the SCT and N de M investment programs for
the period and include (i) acquisition of rails, track
machinery, workshop equipment, locomotive spare parts and
freight wagon and passenger coach components; (ii) major
construction and realignment of rail lines; (iii) consultant
services; and (iv) training equipment and scholarships. The
project will help to accomplish the following objectives:
(i) improve railway capacity; (ii) continue improvements in
railway operating efficiency; (iii) promote financial
self-sufficiency; (iv) strengthen commercial practices; and
(v) upgrade rail infrastructure planning methods.
Beneficiaries: The railways are the principal transport mode for the movement
of important bulk items which are crucial to the Mexican
economy, such as grain, fertilizer, coal, iron ore and
cement. The incidence of transport costs in the final costs
of these products is significant, and, thus, an efficient rail
operation should benefit the economy as a whole and help to
support the economic recovery. Last, the economy as a whole
sho.ld nenefit from the reduced railway burden on the public
sector deficit if operational and financial targets are met.



Risks:          The project faces three risks:
(i) rail traffic projections are subject to substantial
uncertainty as experienced in railway projects all over the
world. Appropriate measures calling for an annual updating of
traffic forecasts and investment requirements have been
incorporated, and financial projections have been based on
more conservative estimates of traffic to ensure an improved
financial position for N de M despite a possible traffic
shortfall;
(ii) Government counterpart funds may be further restricted
during project implementation, but the proposed sector loan
format - allowing for flexibility in investment priorities -
and the agreed minimum outlays for maintenance-related
activities - would minimize the impact of budget cuts;
(iii) it may become difficult for the Government to continue
acting on its policy of reducing subsidies and raising prices
to reflect the cost of services in the face of potential
political resistance. The Government, up to now, has shown a
serious commitment to its stated pricing policies,
particularly in the transport sector.
Estimated
Cost:           Tne total cost of the SCT and N de M rail investment program
for 1985-1988 is estimated at US$2.35 billion equivalent with
a foreign exchange component of US$849 million equivalent.
The Bank loan represents 13% of the total program and 35% of
the foreign exchange requirements.

II. THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
A. Transport Development and the Economy i/
2.01      Mexico has developed an extensive transport network which comprises
over 200,000 km of roads (including 67,000 km of paved and over 80,000 km of
rural roads, with the rest being state and local unpaved roads); about 20,000
km of railways (all except 300 km being standard gauge); some 33 ports, of
which 13 serve international traffic; about 50 airports capable of handling
medium or larger size aircraft; and over 20,000 km of pipelines for crude
oil, refined products and gas. For the 10 years prior to 1982, traffic on
the nation's roads grew by 10% annually, with road traffic representing an
estimated 48% of total traffic in ton-km and over 90% in passenger-km.
Railway freight traffic, representing about 14% of total ton-km, experienced
a growth of 7% annually in that period. Even more significant growth rates
were registered by coastal shipping and pipelines because of increased oil
production, resultinz in estimated shares of 15% and 21% respectively.
After only limited groiwth prior to 1977, the ports registered average annual
increases of almost 17% in cargo tons handled after 1977. In passenger
transport, domestic aviation traffic increased by over 17% annually, with
total passenger-km generally equal to that of the railways.
2.02      During the 1960s and early 1970s, substantial amounts were invested
in transport - particularly roads, railways and aviation - with the transport
sector receiving about 20% of all public investment. With the trunk network
generally in place, emphasis shifted to operatlonal improvements and pricing
and using transport investment to foster the development of outlying areas,
especially through the extensive rural roads construction program of the
mid-1970s. Transport investment declined to 9% of all public investment.
This investment policy, however, proved to be untenable with the surge of
economic activity in the late 1970s, which was accompanied by accelerated
growth in traffic in all modes. Between 1978 and 1981, serious port and rail
bottlenecks emerged, clearly affecting the operation of basic industries and
the conduct of international trade. Consequently, in 1977, the Government
launched an important industrial ports development program and a major track
improvement and modernization plan for the railways. While highways' share
of transport sector investments declined from 68% in 1977 to 44% in 1984,
railways' share grew from 39% to 42% (Table 2.1). Although there were
instances of premature investment and some works suffered from poor
programing, the overall response of the Government to the situation in those
years was appropriate.
1/   The transport sector and, in particular, the highways and ports
subsectors have been treated in more detail in recent Staff Appraisal
Reports for Loans 2428-ME and 2450-ME, both issued in late FY84. Thus,
the discussion of the sector is presented in summary form in this
report.



- 5 -
2.03      With the onset of severe budgetary restrictions from 1982 to the
present, transport investment priorities have shifted again. Transport
investment levels, excluding urban transport, were reduced in 1984, in real
terms, to about US$1.2 billion (roughly equivalent to average 1979-1980
levels), focusing on maintenance, reconstruction of existing infrastructure,
and completion of high priority ongoing works, while deFerring major new
investments. Given the lull in transport demand caused by the recession,
this strategy has been satisfactory for the time being. Rail traffic
declined 12% in 1982; highway traffic, while increasing in 1982, dropped by
10 to 15% in 1983, and several industries, which experienced transport
bottlenecks in the recent past, do not anticipate difficulties in the near
future.
2.04      Projected 1985 transport investment levels stili reflect the
continued restrictive economic situation of the past three years, and no
substantive increases are planned for the sector. As the economy and traffic
begin to recover, however, and many of the major investments initiated before
1982 approach completion, the Government is contemplating new investments in
highways, railways, airports and ports to meet the expected demand. Among
the investments are priority toll road widenings, new rail lines to and along
the Gulf Coast, new railway yards, expanded capacitv for Mexico City airport,
commercial port improvements, and the completion of the industrial port of
Altamira. It remains to be seen whether the economic recovery will be
sufficient and rapid enough to warrant some of these investments. The Bank
sectoral lending program is designed to assist the Government in analyzing
medium- and long-term investments needs through a series of projects in
highways (Loan 2428-ME, FY84), ports (Loan 2450-ME, FY84) and the Railway
Sector Project, the subject of this appraisal report.
2.05      The economic situation has also directed the Government's attention
to pricing and taxation issues which have been of concern to the Bank under
previous projects. In particular, the Government has acted to raise fuel
prices toward international levels; railway tariffs lhave been increased and
costs reduced in real terms, improving the railways' financial position; and
road user tax:es have been increased on the toll road net-,ork.  Further
efforts, however, are still required (para 2.08).
B. Institutional Framework and Planning
2.06      Based on the institutional reforms of the current Administration,
all transport agencies are incorporated under the Secretariat for
Communications and Transport (SCT) (Chart 1). A Subsecretariat For
Infrastructure in SCT handles infrastructure development for almost all
transport, and a Subsecretariat for Operations has responsibility for
operational, regulatory and tariff matters.  A Directorate General for
Planning (DCP), which reports directly to the Secretary, is in charge of
overall planning and budget preparation. Planning units within the various
modal directorates are responsible for identifying and proposing capital
investments and provide the technical details required for the analysis of
such investments. DGP, in turn, has responsibility for carrying out the
economic feasibility analysis and providing a multimodal consistent analytic
framework for the preparation of the medium- and long-term investment
programs. With the recent institutional reorganization of the sector, SCT is
upgrading its planning procedures and criteria, and the Bank is providing



- 6 -
assistance to this effort under various ongoing transport loans. In addition
to SCT, the Secretariat for Programing and Budgeting (SPP) plays an important
role in ensuring consistent modal planning. The Directorate for Public
Infrastructure of SPP must approve the proposed SCT investment and operating
budgets and conducts a serious review of all major investments in the
sector. Although small, this unit has proven effective and capable during
the first years of this Administration.
2.07      To carry out the Government's comprehensive regulation of prices
and operations of the sector, SCT and the Secretariat of Finance (SHCP) share
responsibilities. SCT, through its Directorate General for Tariffs (DGT),
makes the final decision with regard to the pricing of transport services
provided by private operators such as trucking companies and private port
operating firms. For the quasi-public enterprises such as the railways and
public port operating entities, DGT reviews and makes recommendations to the
Pricing Unit of SHCP for final decisions. Other types of regulation of
transport services are carried out by various directorates in SCT under the
Subsecretary of Operations. Although the Bank has expressed concern in the
past over the extensive regulation of transport (including entry, capacity,
route, product and tariff regulation), a recent Bank review of the road
transport industry did not reveal any apparent major distortions and
concluded that, in general, the industry was performing well under the
existing regulatory structure. The review did find that the data base used
by SCT for regulatory decisions was insufficient for the timely
identification of potential transport problems which could require regulatory
change. The Government is now considering the Bank's recommendations and
possible technical assistance under the Second Highway Sector Project to
upgrade the data base.
C. Major Sector Issues
2.08      The major issues in the sector are listed below, and a more
detailed discussion of the issues and the actions being taken is orovided in
Annex 1:
(a) Scope and timing of transport investments. While presently
maintaining a holding strategy on transport investments, the
Government must face major decisions concerning deferred large, new
transport investments. Planning has been complicated by the
uncertainty of the timing, scope and composition of the economic
recovery. Bank lending has been designed to help determine
long-term investment needs, e.g., analytical procedures and
criteria for evaluating investments have been developed and agreed
to under the Second Highway Project (Loan 2428-ME) and under the
proposed Railway Sector Project;
(b)  Diesel prices.  The Government has raised gasoline prices by 247Th,
in real terms, from 1981 to 1985 and diesel prices by 367%. Diesel
prices are now about 77% of international levels (Caribbean FOB
price). The Government continues to move progressively toward
international levels and, in January 1985, established an automatic
monthly increase for diesel prices of 2.5% for the rest of the
year;



(c)   Road user charges for heavy vehicles.  It is apparent  that heavy
vehicles  are not paying sufficiently for the costs they impose upon
the network.   SCT is carrying nuIt the needed studies with  financing
under Loan 2428-ME, and appropriate consultation clauses on this
issue have been agree-d to under that loan;
(d)   Railway financial  situiation.   The  railways have made substantial
progress  in improving their financial situation and have complied
withl financial tairgets under thie Fourth Railway Project (1929-ME);
further measures, however, will be requtired to make the railway a
financially  viable ent-itv as proposed tinder this Sector Project
(Paras 3.144-3.15);
(e)  IPort charsges.  Present port rhlarges do not adequately relate  to
costs and d(i not  re-rover port  infrastructure Investment costs.

specifically cost-related; and an ever-growing debt, 2/ The present project
has taken into account the lessons learned under previous projects.  With
regard to forecasts, in addition to N de M upgrading its forecasting
methodology, a conservative traffic estimate has been applied for the
financial evaluation to determine the ability of N de M to absorb the cost of
a shortfall in expected traffic (para 3.13). Similarly, the sector loan
format will permit an annual re-evaluation of investments and appropriate
adjustments for traffic variations and macro-economic concerns (para 4.05).
Locomotive availability has been evaluated in great detail, and realistic
targets resulting from a comprehensive action plan are proposed (paras 4.19
and 4.26). Finally, substantive action on tariffs has been taken which
confirms the Government's commitment to pricing principles agreed to under
this project (para 3.14).
E. Bank Lending Strategy
2.11      The Bank's role In the transport sector has heen expanded because
of the measures taken bv the Government on issues which have been of concern
to the Bank during its long standing involvement in the sector, particularly
with regard to sector pricing (Annex 1) and institutional reorganization
(para 2.06). The sector lending strategy, in accordance with the Bank's
overall objectives for lending to Mexico, is to develop relatively quick
disbursing loan operations which focus upon short-term investment in
maintenance, rehabilitation and modernization, ai.d upon making prior
investments operational while assisting the Government, through the new
Planning Directorate, in reassessing the medium- and long-term need for major
new investments. Furthermore, a constructive dialogue on sector policies has
been initiated between the Bank and the Government which would be supported
through the hank's lending and sector work program. The Second Highway
Sector Project (Loan 2428-ME) and the Industrial Ports Project (Loan 2450-ME)
have established Bank involvement in the highway and port subsectors. The
proposed Railway Sector Project would complete this comprehensive involvement
by incorporating the railway subsector.
II. THE RAILWAY SUBS.CTOR
A. Organization of the Railwavs
3.01      There are presentlv four Government-owned railways in Mexico, whose
lines are generallv complementary, feeding into one another. N de M Is the
largest of the railways, as shown below (km):
Standard   Narrow
Gauge      Gauge       Total      Z
Nacionales de Mexico          15,024       300       15,324    77.6
Del Pacifico                   2,310        -         2,310    11.7
Chihuahua al Pacifico          1,509        -         1,509     7.6
Sonora-Baja California           610                    610     3.1
Total                    19,453       3019,753   100.0
2/   Project Completion Report for Mexico: Third Railway Proiect, Loan
1232-ME, October 7, 1983.



- 9 -
It operates nearly 80% of the railway network, transporting about 82% of all
freight ton-km and 70% of all passenger-km in 1983, and receives 92% of all
investment (including SCT rail infrastructure).
3.02      In January 1977, a Government decree authorized the integration of
the then five railway enterprises into a single operating unit, and this
process is being accomplished very gradually. The first phase of the
unification has so far been carried out; management has been entrusted to a
single board under the control of a single General Manager. The General
Manager of N de M, thus, nce serves as General Manager of all the railways,
and a management board, comprising representatives of pertinent ministries
and the labor unions, has been established. In 1982, Ferrocarriles Unidos
del Sureste (FUS) was amalgamated with N de M. Furthermore, annual budget
proposals for the four railways are coorainated and prepared by the Planning
Office of N de It. The timing of the amalgariation of the other railways is
uncertain because of the legal implicatio.s, as well as the need to obtain
the approval of the labor unions representing the other railways which
operate under differing work rules and regulations. The Government continues
to proceed cautiously to complete the full amalgamation. The incorporating
of the other railways within N de M should not have a significant impact on
N de M's operations or financial position. It was confirmed at negotiations
that N de M will extend the economic evaluation guidelines agreed to under
the project to the other railways' investment plans as they are incorporated
within N de M. It was also confirmed at negotiations that N de M will
maintain records with respect to project targets (para 4.26) which will
exclude any newly incorporated railways in order to ensure appropriate
monitoring of the project objectives.
3.03      As a state enterprise, the railways are regulated by the
Government, with SCT reviewing annual operating and capital budgets and
controlling tariff changes, the final responsibility being that of the
Secretariat of Finance (SHCP). The main directorates in SCT with regulatory
responsibilities for the railways include the Directorate General of Railway
Operations and the Directorate General of Tariffs (DGT) under the
Subsecretary of Operations (Chart 1). In addition, the construction of new
rail infrastructure, realignments, electrification and yards is handled by
the Directorate General for Railway Infrastructure (DGVF) with financing
provided directly under the SCT budget. The completed works are then turned
over to the railways for operation and maintenance, becoming Go .ernment
equity in the respective railway companies.
B. Management, Staffing and Training
(i) N de M
3.04      In terms of numbers, N de M is adequately staffed (organizational
structure as indicated in Chart 2), wit]. about 64,715 employees (as of
October 1984). As part of the action plans for the previous two railway
projects (Loans 1232-ME and 1929-ME), N de M was encouraged to analyze its
staff requirements and prepare manpower plans which would ensure that, as
traffic increased, essential manpower needs were met, while indiscriminate
additions to staff were avoided. N de M's Plan de Recursos Humanos" has
been reviewed by Bank missions almost every two years since 1978 when it was
first prepared and, during these reviews, there have been detailed
discussions regarding the staff needs of N de M, the requirements of training
and the organizational adjustments necessitated by the increasing demands of



- 10 -
traffic, as well as the progressive modernization of the operations and
technology of N de M. Staff productivity, = measured by traffic units per
employee, improved from 527,000 in 1978 to 641,000 in 1984. The review
conducted during appraisal of the Sector Project forms the basis for the
staff productivity targets included in the Plan of Action (para 4.26).  The
projections for the next five years (Table 3.1) show a modest growth in staff
of about 3%, which, considering the forecast growth in ton-km and
passenger-km over the same -period, implies a 17% growth in efficiency. Staff
productivity is expected to increase to about 750,000 traffic units per
employee by 1988.
3.05      An important element in the effort to improve labor-productivity is
the strategy for training and work rule rationalization agreed to, in
principle, by the Government, N de M and the railway union under the program
for the "Modernization of the National Railway System issued in 1983. The
initial action taken under this program has been the consolidation and
elimination of positions in the railway workshops, some of which dated back
to the days of the steam locomotive and -.lated workshop technology. Along
with this action has been the init'ation of extensive training programs and
an incentive pay arrangement to encourage and upgrade staff. After giving
initial attention to the -srkshops, the modernization program expects to
focus on assessing ways to improve . abor-productivity with regard to line
rehabilitation and maintenance, a- jo renegotiate work rules with regard to
train erews, which will, inter alia, extend the crew operating zones and
lessen the number of crew changes on line, which cause considerable delay and
reduce equipment utilization as well as limit line capacity. These
improvements are taken into consideration in the project Action Plan.
(ii) DGVF
3.06      The Directorate General of Rail Infrastructure (DGVF) of SCT has
had long experience in the design and construction of new railway lines,
major realignments and other infrastructure works. It carries out most of
the engineering and design itself, making use of local firms for some
location, design and drawing work when the voi'me of work warrants it, as
well as specialist contractors for aerial surveys, soils investigations and
material testing.
3.07      For the execution of works, DGVF provides rails, sleepers and
fastenings while the earth works and bridge building works are contracted.
Stone ballast supply and track laying work are also contracted out, either to
the main contractors or to separate contractors, with SCT providing transport
and specialized machinery for ballast handling and track laying. Mexico has
a substantial number of capable contractors who also work on highway
construction. Supervision of the works is carried out by DGVF experts, in
close collaboration with N de M.
C. Achievements in the Railway Subsector
3.08      Substantive improvements in railway operations and finance have
been achieved during the implementation of th_ successive railwav projects
(Table 3.2). In terms of opera:ional efficiency, as mentioned before, labor
productivity has improved such that N de M handled 641,000 units of traffic
per employee in 1984, as compared with 366,C00 in 1970. Annual freight
locomotive-km per locomotive increased from 75,600 to 93,500 during the same
period and passenger locomotive-km from 130,700 to 245,000. Similarly,



- 11 -
average carload increased from 39 to 55 tons per car, and passengers per
coach from 31 to 60. Furthermore, problems which hampered railway efficiency
in the past have been resolved, such as the large number of foreign cars on
line, which reached 27,000 in 1980, straining yard and line capacity; the
number has been reduced to 2,500. Unit trains and direct trains now carry
75% of all ton-km, which, together with the new telecommunications and car
control systems (OCS), helped to reduce car turnaround times from a high of
23 days in 1980 to 17 days in 1984.
3.09      Through these improvements, N de M has been able to serve
increasing traffic (Map IBRD 11844R1).  The railways, despite their low share
in overall traffic (an estimated 14% of all ton-km or 23% of road and rail
ton-km), are crucial to the efficient functioning of several sectors. They
carry about 40% of all beans, about 30% of all sorghum, about 50% of all
wheat and about 10% of all corn produced and imported by Mexico. The
railways are also the principal carriers for iron ore, coke, coal and sugar
and are major carriers for fertilizers, cement and many steel products.
N de M's traffic generally has followed the growth in GDP. From 1970 to
1984, both GDP and railway freight ton-km increased by an average annual rate
of about 5.0%. N de M's freight traffic grew from 39.1 million tons and 18.4
billion ton-km in 1970 to an estimated 61.9 million tons and 37.1 billion
Lon-km by 1984, with ton-km growing faster than tons (5.0% compared with
about 3.0%) as average hauls increased from 470 to about 600 km per ton
during the 1970-1984 period (Table 3.3). In contrast to the worldwide
trend, Mexico's railway traffic has increased steadily, and railway's share
in the transport of its principal commodities has not changed significantly,
except in the case of petroleum and derivatives, for which pipelines have
replaced rail services. Excluding petroleum products, the historic annual
growth for N de M tons and ton-km from 1970 to 1984 is 3.7% and 5.77
respectively.
3.10      On the financial side, after years of inadequate tariff increases,
the Government took action to reduce transport subsidies and raised rail
freight tariffs by 40% from 1980 to 1984 in real terms (Table 3.4). The
higher tariffs, together with the increasing traffic (para 3.09) and reduced
unit costs (due to improved efficiency and real reductions in wages and
materials costs) resulted in working and operating ratios in 1983 (and
apparently similar or better ratios in 1984) of 0.85 and 1.08 respectively,
surpassing financial targets in the Fourth Railway Project 3/. These ratios
compare with working and operating ratios of 1.38 and 1.60 respectively in
1980 (see Annex 2 for more details).
D. Areas of Concern in the Subsector
3.11      The following discussion summarizes the main problem areas
concerning the railways, which have been the main focus of project
preparation efforts and the action plan (paras 4.1>-4.26).
(i) Freight Traffic Forecast and Commercial Practices
3.12      Previous traffic forecasts prepared by N de M, in conjunction with
Bank projects, have proven to be over-optimistic, 7.6-7.8% annual gr^-ith
projections compared with 3.8-4.6% actual, mainly due to two factors which
make railway freight forecasting difficult in Mexico. The first is the
3/   Including Government specific compensation for passenger services and
reimbursement for pension payments as N de M revenue.



- 12 -
volatility of the Mexican economy. Both the Third and Fourth Railway
Projects covered periods of severe economic recession in 1976 and 1982. The
second factor is the uncertainty regarding grain imports. The railways carry
a high percentage of grain imports so that, in years of poor harvests, rail
tonnage increases, while, in years of good harvests, rail tonnage declines;
Mexico, thus, revises its forecasts e-very year and adjusts its investment
plans, particularly for locomotives and rolling stock. To minimize the
problem of uncertainty, the project proposes the following with regard to
rail traffic forecasts: forecasting methodology and data base have been
upgraded, particularly for grain; an annual review process is being
institutionalized; and two forecasts have been prepared - a commercial
forecast following N ae M's detailed product-by-product methodology and a low
forecast for the financial analysis based on the historical trend of the last
14 years. The commercial forecast has been applied to determine investment
requirements to ensure that the railways have sufficienL capacity to serve
the identified target market. The low forecast has been used in the
financial projection to ensure that N de M will be able to maintain an
adequate financial position even if investments are made and expected traffic
does not materialize (more details on the traffic forecast are provided in
Annex 3).
3.13      The commercial forecast for 1984-1988 predicts an annual increase
in tonnage of 5.3% and in ton-km of 5.0%, rising from 61.9 to 76.2 million
tons and 37.1 to 45.2 billion ton-km (Table 3.5). The distance per ton is
expected to stabilize at about 593 km. Major traffic increases are expected
for certain grains and intermediate industrial products for which N de M
believes it can attain a higher market share and is targeting special
commercial efforts. The low" forecasL, which is applied in the financial
evaluation, assumes an annual growth rate of about 3.0% for tonnage, reaching
66.6 million tons or 41.8 billion ton-km by 1988. Related to this forecast
is the need.for improved and enhanced marketing practices. In order to
pursue a more aggressive commercial policy, the project proposes a
restructuring of the Traffic Department's marketing function and the testing
of new marketing approaches. Appropriate actions are called for in the
Action Plan (para 4.21), including more detailed costing studies for railway
operations, i.e., workshops, and specific commodity movements, i.e., the
cost of hackhaul movements. Technical assistance will he provided umder the
project to support these efforts.
(ii) Financial Status
3.14      Although the overall working and operating ratios of N de M have
improved significantly, further actions will be required leading toward the
objective of financial viability for N de M (for more details on N de M's
financial status, see Annex 2).  While tariff adjustments since 1982 have
managed to keep freight revenues ahead of inflationary cost increases, a
number of important commodities, e.g., iron ore, coal, ccke and cement, were
still, at the end of 1984. being carried at tariffs which, while covering
short-rln variable costs, are below long-term variable costs (with the latter
being distinguished from the former by including depreciation and interest)
(Table 3.6). It was agreed at negotiations that, as a special condition of
effectiveness, the Government and N de M would furnish a p'an of action
satisfactory to the Bank for the tariff reclassification of commodities and
would take initial action to reclassify some of the commodities still carried
at tariffs below long-term variable costs, which, de facto, represents an
important step toward the ultimate goal of covering such costs. At
negotiations, it was also agreed that, by January 1988, tariffs for all rail
commodities would be at least equal to long-term variable costs.



- 13 -
3.15      The other financial issue relates to N de M's debt situation.
Interest charges, which are mainly on foreign loans, have more than doubled
ir real terms over the last five years and, in 1983, consumed 46% of gross
revenue. Government and railway representatives, in response to this
problem, agreed that part of the long-term debt, related to N de M's need to
borrow in order to cover operating cash shortfalls, would be transferred to
the Government. Such deficits resulted from the Government's refusal to
allow adequate increases in tariffs, keeping revenues from rising
sufficiently to meet inflationary increases in costs and leaving N de M to
borrow on current account. The related borrowing amounts to about 40% of the
existing long-term debt of N de M by December 1983. An equivalent amount of
such debt would be converted, in stages, into Government equity during three
years beginning in 1985, leaving the railway with a debt burden based upon
capital borrowing, which could, in the future, be serviced by N de M itself,
if other financial conditions of the project are fulfilled (paras 4.20
and 4.26). Agreement on the debt assumption has been finalized, and initial
action has been taken for debt service payments in 1985.
(iii) Locomotive Efficiency
3.16      During recent years, N de M has been struggling with the problem of
declining availability of the diesel fleet (about 1,600 units in the fleet),
together with a steady increase in its traffic. To some extent, the two
phenomena have been interconnected as traffic pressures have led to neglect
of maintenance. Other factors, however, have also been responsible for the
development of a situation that requires an urgent solution. During the
sharp upsurge of traffic in 1979-1981, N de M hired and purchased second-hand
locomotives from the U.S. railroads, which are still in service. In
addition, because of budget limitations and import restrictions, the ordering
of spare parts did not keep pace with the requirements, while the number of
over-aged units has been increasing (Table 3.7). The Bank, therefore,
requested N de M to make an in-depth review of the locomotive situation and
to prepare an integrated locomotive recovery plan comprising: (a) a scrapping
program (218 locomotives to be eliminated in the years 1985-1988);
(b) a rehabilitation program for suitable locomotives which can be
reconditioned; (c) a purchase program for spare parts and components, and
equipment to streamline workshop repairs; and (d) purchase of new
locomotives. The plan also includes a review of workshop practices and
training programs (see Annex 4 for more details). The implementation of the
locomotive program has already started and is showing results. The
availability of locomotives improved from 65.5% in 1983 to 68% in 1984.
Under the project, availability is projected to rise to 80% by 1988
(para 4.26). The overall effect of the locomotive program will be that the
diesel locomotive fleet will be reduced, from 1,614 in 1984 to 1,457 in 1988,
with substantial increases in locomotive availability and in traffic served.
(iv) Track Condition
3.17      N de M operates about 15,300 km of track, of which some 1,250 km
were added in 1982 when FUS was integrated with the N de M system. N de M
faces two problems related to track infrastructure: high operating costs and
capacity limitations due to excessive gradients and curvatures on main lines;
and increasing accidents due to track conditions. Track-related accidents
are up by 22%, as compared with a decline of 45% in operations-related



- 14 -
accidents from 1980 to 1983 (Table 3.8). Regarding accidents, poor
maintenance and fluctuations in the rate of track rehabilitation are of
considerable concern. N de M has concluded that its labor-intensive
maintenance practices are ineffective in meeting the increasing maintenance
requirements as traffic rises. As a result, N de M is pursuing an
accelerated mechanization program with substantial equipment purchases
beginning in 1985 and a reorganization of labor (see Annex A for more
details). Appropriate maintenance targets for 1985-1988 are presented in
paragraph 4.26. Also, rail rehabilitation efforts have fluctuated with
budgetary allocations and the Government's policy on imports. During the
height of the recession, approval of rail imports was delayed, limiting track
rehabilitation. For 1985, N de M expects to carry out deferred
rehabilitation by completing about 340 km of track rehabilitation with new
rail and about 260 km with recovered rail (see Annex 4 for more details).
Appropriate rehabilitation targets for 1986-1988 are presented in
paragraph 4.26.
3.18      Resolving the problem of excessive gradients and curvature on trunk
lines is the focus of the SCT infrastructure works being carried out by
DGVF (see Annex 5 for more details). Many of these realignment works were
reviewed during the appraisal of the Third and Fourth Railway Projects, but
have progressed at a slower-than-expected rate, mainly because of budgetary
restrictions. The realignment works are generally concentrated on the
,iexico-Veracruz, Cordoba-Coatzacoalcos, Mexico-Lazaro Cardenas and
Saltillo-Monterrey lines.  Substantial capacity additions are being provided
through the double-tracking of Mexico-Irapuato. Finallv, new lines have been
initiated between Guadalajara and Monterrey and hetween Veracruz and
Tampico. Traffic forecasts and costs liave clhanged significantly since these
works were initiated, and a re-evaluation made during preparation of the
Sector Project confirmed the feasibility of most of the program. A number of
works, hnwever, are being delayed or scaled-down (para 4.33).
(v) Yard Capacity
3.19      The installation of telecommunications and the operational control
system have helped to reduce car turnaround times to 17 days in 1984 from a
high of 23 days in 1980 (Table 3.2), but this level is still considered
high.   The capacity constraints being experienced at the main railway yards
in Mexico City, Monterrey, Guadalajara and Coatzacoalcos represent a
principal factor in the long turnaround times for wagons. Freight wagons
generally spend a major portion of their available time in yards, as compared
with the line-haul movement. The physical expansion of existing yards,
however, is limited because these yards are now located within urban
centers. With restricted budgets, major yard works have been postponed, but
a Master Plan Study of all N de M vards is to be initiated to review the
operations of existing yards, to determine the future workload of the yards,
to view the problem of capacity on a network basis and to appropriately
locate, design and stage new yard investments (para 4.24).
(vi) Passenger Services
3.20      As the financial performance of N de M's freight services has
improved, attention is focusing upon N de M's passenger services, which
continue to generate deficits, while levels of service, in terms of equipment
and on-time performance, deteriorate. N de M offers a number of inter-urban



- 15 -
passenger services, including sleeping car service, first cl.lass special,
first class and second class services, and a mixed freight .-nd passenger
train service for outlying low density lines. Passenger traffic had declined
from over 33.2 million passengers in 1970 to 16 million passengers in 1981
until recent increases returned traffic levels to 22 million in 1983
(includinTg 3 million passengers of the recently incorporated FUS line). Much
of the decline is due to the elimination of uneconomic services representing
43% of all passenger services between 1974 and 1978. Passenger-km, however,
have not declined as precipitously because the average distance per passenger
rose from about 100 km to 190 km from 1970 to 1983, and, today, N de M
carries 4.2 billioa passenger-km, as compared with 3.4 billion in 1970.
3.21      Passenger fares cover only about 60% of short-run variable costs,
and the Government has been reluctant to raise fares substantially in real
terms, despite the substantial differential between bus and rail fares,
because service is so bad and because 87% of the passengers are lower income
groups using second class service. Unit cost decreases, higher load factors
per coach and per train, and the reduction of uneconomic services, together
with some real fare increases, however, reduced the deficit in real terms,
which is covered by a specific Government payment to N de M, by 37% from 1980
to 1984, reaching US$26 million equivalent per year.
3.22      The Government and N de M have proposed a comprehensive strategy
for passenger services which includes a new fare policy to reduce subsidies,
the improvement of high density services, the analysis of the potential for
new first class services on main lines which are being upgraded and the -
gradual elimination of low density services for which no social or economic
justification can be made (see Annex 6 for more details). The main element
in this strategy is the investment in new coaches beginning with the
acquisition of replacement coaches for existing services, followed by the
acquisition of additional coaches for new first-class services once such
services are shown to be economically and financially viable. Most of the
present fleet of coaches are over 25 years old, with more than 200 coaches
over 45 years old (Table 3.7). Based on this strategy, N de M has forecast
that total passenger trips (excluding the introduction of new services) will
grow from 22.5 million in 1984 to 24.8 million in 1988 and passenger-km from
4.3 billion in 1984 to 5.0 billion in 1988 for an average annual growth of
2.5% and 3.8% respectively (Table 3.9). The forecast assumes that all growth
will occur on the high density lines, while the lower density lines' traffic
either remains stable or declines.
3.23      In implementing the proposed strategy, the Bank has recommended a
series of actions to be taken, including a line-by-line costing exercise, the
raising of fares to long-term variable costs, and procedures for the economic
evaluation of new coach acquisitions. Initial estimates confirm the economic
viability of most of the existing inter-urban services. N de M's initial
coach acquisition contract for 1985 and 1986, however, contrary to the
proposed strategy (para 3.22), provides for more first-class "special"
coaches than required for replacement, and the economic evaluation is not
sufficient. N de M is now negotiating to postpone the 1986 tranche of such
coaches until the economic and financial Justification can be confirme'
(para 4.29).



- 16 -
(vii) Management Training
3.24      Although N de M has enjoyed a measure of stability in its
management unusual for railroads in Latin America, there is a lack of young
middle-management personnel required for continuity and the adoption of new
management techniques. While substantial attention has been paid to the
training needs of line personnel, the need for developing a cadre of young
professionals for management is one that assumes paramount importance with
the integration of the other railways and the gradually increasing workload
of the new system. To meet this need, the project includes a scheme for the
upgrading of qualified railway personnel and for the recruitment of
university graduates who would receive theoretical training at university and
practical training online at N de M, as well as overseas with railways
abroad. Financing for the foreign-exchange costs of the program would be
provided from the proposed loan (para 4.17).
IV. THE RAILWAY ACTION PLAN AND THE PROJECT
A.   The Project Concept and Objectives
4.01      The Railway Sector Project (Fifth Railway Project) will extend Bank
support for the continued improvement of the Mexican railways and will
broaden Bank participation in Mexico's transport sector following on the
Second Highway Sector Project (2428-ME) and the Lazaro Cardenas Industrial
Ports Project (2450-ME), both approved in FY84. The project will provide
assistance to help finance the 1985-1988 time-slice of the N de M and SCT
railway investment program and, by including financing for SCT works, it
represents a wider involvement than that under previous projects. The
long-standing relationship between N de M, SCT and the Bank, and the
experience gained through a series of consecutive transport projects, make it
possible to apply a sector loan format to this project, with the
responsibility for appraising investment commitments beyond the first year
resting with the staff of N de M and SCT, following procedures agreeable to
the Bank (Annexes 7 and 8).
4.02      The overall objective of the project is to support Government and
railway efforts to implement a Plan of Action wXhich will:
(a) improve railway traffic carrying capacity through a balanced
investment program;
(b) continue improvements in railway operating efficiency through
higher productivity of equipment and human resources;
(c) promote financial self-sufficiency for railway services with
tariffs appropriately related to railway costs and a rationalized
debt structure;
(d)  strengthen cosmercial practices for railway services; and
(e) upgrade planning methods for assessing railway infrastructure
requirements.



- 17 -
B.   Investment Program 1985-1988
(i) Overall Program
4.03      Since 1977, rail investment has increased substantially such that,
in 1984, total rail investmeni, including DGVF and all four railways, was 95Z
above the 1977 level, reaching about US$525 million equivalent in the
1980-1982 period (Table 4.1). The main increase was in new construction, up
over 200% in real terms from 1977 to 1984, due to the double-tracking and
electrification of Mexico-Queretaro and the new sections on the
Mexico-Veracruz line. Similarly, track rehabilitation increased by about
180%. Locomotive purchases have fluctuated, and wagon acquisitions, although
increased in the 1980-1982 period, are now at the levels they were in 1977.
The major decline has been for signaling, telecommunications, workshop and
track machinery and passenger coaches, all of which receive increasing
allocations in the projected 1985-1988 investment program. Prior Bank
reviews have confirmed the economic soundness of these investments. In some
cases, e.g., electrification works, the acquisition of machinery and
materials was premature because track works had not been completed. Also,
wagon and locomotive purchases were higher than would have been the case if
wagon turnaround times were lower and locomotive availability were higher.
4.04      The total N de M and SCT railway investment program for 1985-1988
is estimated to cost US$2,354 million equivalent (including physical and
price contingencies), of which N de M represents US$1,325 million equivalent
and SCT represents US$1,029 million equivalent (Table 4.2). The foreign
exchange cost of the total program, including the indirect foreign exchange
for SCT rail infrastrtucture civil works, is estimated at US$849 million
equivalent, of which N de M represents US$457 million equivalent and SCT
represents USS392 million equivalent.
4.05      A detailed analysis of the 1985 investment proposals and a more
general review of the proposals for subsequent years were carried out. It
was confirmed at negotiations that, by November of each year, beginning in
1985, N de M and SCT will prepare, for Bank comment, an updated four-year
investment program with a detailed breakdown for the following year based
uyon agreed technical and economic criteria. No investment should be included
unless it has an estimated rate of return of at least 12%.
(ii) N de M Investment
4.06      General.  The N de M program is about US$300 million equivalent per
year (ir 1985 prices), compared with about US$370 million equivalent in the
1980-1982 period, with a direct foreign exchange content of about 34%
(Table 4.3). The composition of the program is summarized in the following
paragraphs, and a more detailed discussion is provided in Annexes 4 and 7.
The investments are weighted heavily toward track maintenance and
rehabilitation, with all track-related investments reaching 28% of total
investments.



- 18 -
4.07      Track.  The track rehabilitation program with new rail is expected
to cover an average of 360 km per year at an average cost per km of about
US$150,000 equivalent per km (in mid-1985 prices) (Map IBRD 18768). Track
rehabilitation on lower traffic density lines with recovered rail should
cover 223 km per year over the period at an average cost of US$30,000
equivalent per km (Table 4.4). In addition, N de M has planned to purchase
about US$71 million equivalent worth of track machinery (in mid-1985 prices),
about 45% of this in 1985, as part of its plans to mechanize track
maintenance (Table 4.5).
4.08       Signaling.  In pursuance of Its policy of providing CTC on the
heavily used single-lines, a plan foreseei in the Fourth Railway Project but
inadequately implemented so far because of the lack of financial resources,
N de M proposes the following works in 1985: completion of CTC works in hand
between Guadalajara and Irapuato (250 km); installation of CTC between San
Luis Potosi and Benjamin Mendez (345 km); and completion of the study for CTC
between Lecheria-Teotihuacan-Vera Cruz (404 km) and initiation of the
purchase of materials. In addition, the 1985 budget provides for the
renovation and modernization of the over-aged car control and signaling
equipment in the Valle de Mexico marshaling yard to make the system serve the
longer and heavier cars now in use. The continuation of CTC works in
1986-1988 is likely to be confined to the completion of the above-mentioned
works.
4.09      Telecommunications.  N de M is currently making an analysis of the
combined Telecommunications and Operational Control System installed over the
network under the Third and Fourth Railway Projects. Work in 1985 to 1988
will comprise extension of the telecommunications system to the former FUS
Railway and, later, to the other three Railways - Pacifi_z, Ulihuahua and
Sonora-Baja California.  The main work for 1985 compiLses purchase of
additional V1F radios for 700 locomotives, and for track and train crews, as
well as the extension of telephone exchange and teleprintez facilities.
4.10      Motive Power.  As efficiency, particularly locomotive availability,
improves In accordance with the Plan of Action (para 4.26), N de M will
minimize its locomotive purchase (Table 4.6). In 1985 and 1986, about 20 new
locomotives are expected to be acquired each year at a cost of US$1.2 million
per unit. Also, 20 existing locomotives are scheduled for rehabilitation
each year, at a cost of US$230,000 per unit. In 1987 and 1988, no new
locomotives should be required since 39 electric locomotives, already
acquired for the Mexico-Irapuato line, will come into operation. N de M is
also expected to build up its locomotive spare parts inventory, which had
been depleted in previous years.
4.11      Rolling Stock.  In accordance with the traffic forecasts and the
target indices for wagon turnaround, N de M intends to purchase 460 wagons in
1985 (Table 4.7) and an additional 1,500 wagons per year from 1986 to 1988
(Table 4.8), at an average unit cost of US$65,000 equivalent. The railway
also intends to rehabilitate 700 wagons in 1985 and a similar number annually
thereafter, at an average cost of US$35,000 equivalent. Furthermore, N de M
has initiated the purchase of 330 passenger coaches, beginning with 30
first-class special coaches in 1985, at an average cost of US$500,000
equivalent each.



- 19 -
(iii) SCT Investment
4.12      The SCT program is about US$230 million equivalent per year (in
1985 prices), with a direct and indirect foreign exchange content of about
38% (Table 4.9). The composition of the program is summarized below, and a
more detailed discussion is provided in Annexes 5 and 8. About 67% of the
line works and electrification investments in the 1985-1988 program are
directed at completing ongoing works. The main items in the program
(Map IBRD 18767) include:
(a) the completion of double-tracking, with electrification on the
Mexico City-Queretaro-Irapuato line;
(b) the regrading and realignment of the Mexico City-Veracruz line and
sections between Veracruz and Coatzacoalcos;
(c) the completion of a new line between Guadalajara and Monterrey to
facilitate direct traffic between Manzanillo and Monterrey, and the
construction of appropriate bypasses around Guadalajara;
(d) the construction of rail bypasses at Monterrey and Saltillo; and
(e) the completion of realignment works between Mexico City and Lazaro
Cardenas.
4.13      Provision has been made for the continuation of a new line between
Veracruz and Tampico and the construction of a new line between Mexico City
and Tampico. Major investment in these subprojects, except for contracts
which already have been initiated, has been postponed pending further studies
(para 4.33), and priority has been placed upon directing limited budgets
toward the completion of works well under way. There is also a budgctary
allocation in later years for major yard investment, depending upon the
results of the proposed studies (para 4.24).
4.14      DGVF has also proposed the acquisition of specialized machinery
(Table 4.10) in order to carry out the construction program effectively.
This will require a total of about US$10 million equivalent (in 1985 prices)
between 1986 and 1988.

BANOBRAS as the borrower, with SCT and N de M as the executing agencies and
approximately US$150 million allocated to each entity. The loan would
represent 33% of the estimated foreign exchange requirements of N de M and
38% of the estimated foreign exchange requirements of SCT. The main
components for Bank financing would be as follows (in millions of US$,
including price contingencies):

D.   Plan of Action
(i)  Actions to Be Taken
4.19       In preparing this project, the railways and the Government, in
consultation with the Bank, established a series of actions directed at
resolving the major issues discussed in this report and a set of operational,
investment and financial targets to be achieved during the 1985-1988 period.
4/  This is net of that part of the existing debt 'o he assumed bv the
Government.
5/  Includes the balance of Loan 1929-ME still to he disbursed - about
USS45 million.



- 22 -
4.20      Financial Status.  To meet the financial targets in paragraph 4.26,
it was agreed at negotiations that:
(a) by no later than January 1988, tariffs for each commodity will be
at a level to cover at least the long-term variable costs;
(b) a detailed costing of mainline passenger services will be carried
out by N de M, distinguishing between lines and types of services,
by November 1986;
(c) the accounting systems will be modernized to enable costing,
budgeting and other management information to be produced through
electronic data processing by December 1987;
(d) passenger fares will be raised to long-term variable costs by
January 1988, except in the case of second-class passenger services
for which economic and social considerations miglht warrant fares
set below such levels; and
(e)  Government compensation for passenger services will br paid in a
timely manner and will cover related losses.
4.21       Commercial Practices and Costing.  To attain forecast traffic
levels and facilitate tariff estimates, it was agreed at negotiations that:
(a)  N de M will establish an accounting mechanism to monitor workshop
costs by September 1987;
(b) N de M will carry out specific costing exercises for a number of
important product movements and for backhaul services bv March
1986;
(c) N de M will study the organization and staffing of its commercial
department and make proposals for its restructuring by September
1986; and
(d)  N de M will study and prepare a marketing strategy by no later than
December 1986 and begin its application no later than April 1987.
4.22      Locomotive and Wagon Utilization.  To achieve operational targets
for locomotive and wogon utilization, it was agreed at negotiations that:
(a)  sufficient budgetary allocations will he provided annually to
ensure the needed spare parts for locomotive maintenance;
(b)  an agreed locomotive scrapping program will he executed between
1985 and 1988;
(c)  the operational control system (OCS) will be fully installed and
activated by June 1986 and, by December 1986, a report on its
Initial utilization and impact and future applications will be
prepared and discussed with the Bank; and
(d) a program will be introduced to monitor the cost and productivitv
of rehabilitated locomotives.



- 23 -
4.23      Track Maintenance.  To meet the need for improved track
maintenance, it was agreed at negotiations that:
(a)  sufficient budgetary allocations will be provided annually to
ensure adequate track maintenance and the oaintenance of track
machinery; and
(b)  N de M will monitor and report annually, beginning in 1986, on the
productivity of newly mechanized maintenance units.
4.24      Line and Yard Capacity.  To help assess capacity limitations, it
was agreed at negotiations that:
(a)  N de N will install and train on a Train Performance Calculator by
June 1987; and
(b)  N de M will carry out a master plan study for future yards by
September 1986.
4.25      Training.  It was agreed at negotiations that N de M will carry out
a middle-management training program (para 3.24).

E.   Financial Forecasts
4.27      Financial projections for N de M over the period 1984-1988 are
given in detail in the second part of Annex 2 and the accompanying tables.
Briefly summarized, the revenue projections are based on a continuation of
the historic trend of traffic tonnage growth, some 3% per year, on the
assumption that the Government will indeed permit an annual average increase
in real freight tariffs of no less than 2% and that tariffs known not to be
covering long-term variable costs at present will be duly adjusted by March
1986 at the latest. With regard to passengers, the present level of specific
passenger compensation to cover the approximately 40% of costs not covered by
fares has been assumed, even though the Government agreed, at negotiations,
to progressively raise fares and reduce subsidies. On the cost side, the
wage awards of 1984, increased manpower projections and the incentive scheme
under the Thedernization Plan have been taken into account. In addition,
the Government will take over some 47% of the existing long-term debt,
reimburse N de M for direct pension payments still to be made to retired
staff, and compensate N de M for losses related to passerLer services.
4.28      Under such circumstances, N de M would be able to achieve an
operating ratio of less than 1.00 by 1985 and would, in addition, be able to
service all its own debt and still have some cash reserve left over by 1988.
The following is a summary of the projected Income Accounts and relevant
ratios for the period 1984-1988.

F.   Economic Evaluation
(i) General
4.29      A set of ec&rLomic evaluation procedures and guidelines has been
prepared by N de M and SCT in consultation with the Bank. These guidelines
have been applied in analyzing the investment commitments for 1985, whether
or not Bank financing is involved, and will be applied in annual updatings of
the four-year investment program and the annual preparation of the investment
budget (para 4.05). The results show that the proposed 1985 rail investments
have estimated rates of return well above 12% (Table 4.12) and indicate the
economic feasibility of the proposed 1986-1988 investment. A few low-return
subprojects in the SCT program have been postponed pending further review.
An initial evaluation of the acquisition of passenger coaches indicated a
serious need and acceptable rate of return for replacement coaches, but did
not support the railway's recent contracting for 100 first-class special
coaches to be delivered in 1985 and 1986. Traffic is insufficient, and there
is a more substantial need for first and second-class replacement coaches.
Prior to negotiations, N de M reviewed its initial acquisition of first-class
special passenger coaches; it is attempting to limit the order to those
already set for delivery in 1985 and is considering the acquisition of first
or second-class coaches for 1986. Further purchases of first-class special
coaches should await appropriate marketing studies which take into account
the effects of the proposed tariff policy upon demand forecasts and confirm
the economic and financial feasibility of such coaches. The proposed
guidelines and the results for 1985 are described in Annexes 7 and 8.
4.30      The principal economic benefits of the rail investments are
(a) savings in rail operating costs as track conditions are improved and
train delays are reduced; and (b) savings from avoiding the diversion of
traffic to higher cost road transport by ensuring sufficient capacity for
existing and future traffic levels. A comparison of road and rail economic
transport costs reflecting the net difference in door-to-door costs, with
fuel prices adjusted to reflect international prices, showed that road
freight transport has a long-term variable economic cost per ton-km of about
Mex$ 3.95 (in 1983 prices), compared with a long-term variable rail cost of
Mex$ 1.50 per ton-km. For passengers, the long-term variable road cost is
about Mex$ 3-4 per pass-km, compared with rail costs, ranging from Hex$ 2 to
Mex$ 7 per pass-km, depending upon the type of service offered and the
traffic density on the line. Appropriate sensitivity analyses have been
applied in order to ensure investment viability despite uncertainty over
several parameters, i.e., the timing and magnitude of traffic diversion. The
results confirmed the economic feasibility of the evaluated items.
(ii) N de M
4.31      The analysis of the 1985 investment commitments in the N de M
program covers track rehabilitation works, signaling, locomotive and wagon
acquisition and rehabilitation and passenger coach acquisition. In addition,
track machinery and workshop equipment acquisitions have been incorporated
into the overall evaluation of the track rehabilitation works and locomotive
and wagon investments respectively. The total of the evaluated items
represents 87% of the 1985 investment budget for N de M, and the rates of
return ranged from 18% to 74%. Investment in telecommunications equipment,
representing 5% of the 1985 investment budget, relates to an overall
telecommunications program prepared and evaluated under a previous Bank



- 26 -
project. In this case, a technical review of the items to be financed was
carried out, but an economic evaluation was not considered necessary.
4.32      To confirm the scale and types of benefits being estimated for
N de M investments, a series of monitoring efforts are included in the Plan
of Action, particularly with regard to track maintenance and locomotive
rehabilitation (paras 4.22-4.23).
(iii) SCT
4.33      The analysis of the 1985 investment program for SCT infrastructure
works focused on ongoing as well as new works. The issue to be resolved was
whether subprojects initiated in previous years, most of which were reviewed
during the appraisal of the Fourth Railway Project, are still viable taking
into consideration cost increases and lower traffic forecasts since that
time. Much of the rail to be acquired and some of the civil works to be
tendered are for these ongoing subprojects. Tne economic evaluation provided
estimated rates of return with and without sunk costs. The results confirmed
the feasibility of most of the works, with rates of return ranging from 16%
to 56%. The double-tracking which was initiated between Ramos Arizpe and
Villa Garcia (US$43 million equivalent in 1985 prices) does not provide a
sufficient rate of return, and the works have been discontinued. In
addition. with regard to the Veracruz-Tampico new line (US$23 million
equivalent budgeted for 1985-1988), investment is being limited to ongoing
contracts until further studies clarify the economic feasibility of this
subproject.
4.34      To improve the analyses of capacity and the comparison of
alternative operational and capital improvements to expand line and yard
capacity, measures have been included in the Plan of Action (para 4.24).
G. Procurement, Disbursements and Auditing
4.35      Procedures for project implementation, including annual investment
plan update and review, procurement procedures, disbursement rates and
schedule and auditing requirements, are detailed in Annex 9. All items to be
procured and the civil works to be contracted using the proceeds of this loan
would be subject to international competitive bidding (ICB) as conducted
under the ongoing Fourth Railway Project (1929-ME) and the Second Highway
Sector Project (2428-ME), all in accordance with current Bank guidelines.
For goods and equipment, local manufacturers would be allowed a preferential
margin of 15% of the CIF cost of competing imports, or the relevant
prevailing level of customs duties, whichever is lower. For goods having an
estimated contract value equal to or less than US$500,000 equivalent, tender
documents and proposed awards would not be subject to prior review provided
that the aggregate of such contract amounts does not exceed US$50 million
equivalent. For goods having an estimated contract value of more than
US$500,000 equivalent and all remaining goods contracts, all draft tender
documents and proposed awards would be subject to prior review. All civil
works draft tender documents and procedures would be subject to prior
review. However, because of its proven capabilities and long experience with
the Bank, SCT would award and sign civil works contracts without Bank prior
review. Consultant services and related equipment would be acquired in
accordance with Bank guidelines.



- 27 -
4.36      The design and specifications of the freight cars to be
manufactured by CN are considered satisfactory. However, the proposed design
for the passenger coaches is relatively old in terms of current practice in
the rest of the world, where tubular, all-welded, anti-telescopic designs are
being used which are not only lighter but also relatively safer in case of
accidents. The Bank has, therefore, recommended to N de M and CN that, with
the substantial number of passenger coaches to be manufactured in the next
few years, CN should explore the possibility of modernizing the design of the
coach and importing the required technology to manufacture the new design.
If, therefore, this change of design can be accomplished without an
uneconomic increase in the price of the coach due to the change in
technology, it would be desirable to adopt the revised designs. It was
agreed at negotiations that N de M and CN will explore the possibility of
changeover to a more modern design for the passenger coach in the future and
review the matter with the Bank by December 31, 1985. Also, to ensure a
competitive overall cost for the coaches (for which the Bank is financing
onLly imported components), it was agreed at negotiations that N de M will
establish, by December 31, 1985, a system, acceptable to the Bank, for the
monitoring of costs of the passenger coaches to be acquired.
4.37      For imported goods, disbursements would be made on the basis of CIF
costs, and, with respect to bids won by local manufacturers under ICB,
disbursements would be made on the basis of 100% of the ex-factory costs.
With regard to civil works, the Bank would finance 100% of the estimated
direct and indirect foreign exchange costs, amounting to 35% of the total
cost of each work (excluding value added taxes, IVA) or 100% of the direct
foreign exchange expenditures. 7/ The Bank would also finance 100% of the
cost of consultant services and 100% of the foreign exchange costs for
equipment, computer software and scholarships under the technical assistance
and training programs. Disbursements for civil works would be made on the
basis of statements of expenditures. The estimated disbursement schedule for
the proposed loan, based on the assumption that the loan would become
effective by November 1985, is shown in Table 4.13, and is based on the
standard IBRD profile for railway projects. The closing date would be
June 30, 1992. It was agreed at negotiations that SCT would complete all
investments initiated under the project in a timely manner and would provide
the funds, facilities and other resources required.
4.38      Auditing would be conducted annually by independent auditors for
N de M and SCT accounts as currently agreed under ongoing projects. In
addition, it was agreed at negotiations that SCT and N de M would prepare
periodic reports for the Bank in accordance with an agreed schedule and
format covering the progress of the project and the Plan of Action
(paras 4.19-4.26) and that a project completion report (PCR) would be
prepared within six months of the closing date. It should be noted that,
because of the comprehensiveness of this project, it would require more
supervision than the average for the sector, estimated at about 26
staff-weeks per year.
7/ The foreign exchange component for civil works is based on a detailed
consultant study, contracted by the Government, which analyzed the
composition of the various items and tasks in railway construction works.



- 28 -
H.   Assessment of Risk
4.39      The Railway Sector Project has been prepared on the basis of the
experiences of the previous four railway projects and has been designed in
accordance with the present economic circumstances of Mexico and the
projected outlook for 1985-1988. Two risks are related to the investment
plan: that projected traffic may not materialize, as was the case under
previous projects; or that Government budgetary allocations may be further
restricted and limit the amount of railway investment. With regard to
traffic, the average rate of growth projected is well below the estimates of
previous projects (5.1% per year, compared with about 7.6-7.8%). Also, in
previous projects, the economic evaluation of investments has been subject to
sensitivity tests in order to ensure feasibility under varying
circumstances. In addition, annual updating of investment plans will be
carried out in accordance with updated traffic estimates. Last, the
financial evaluation is based on a low" traffic estimate to confirm that
premature investments which may occur would not have a significant impact
upon the financial status of N de M. With regard to concerns on the
availability of counterpart fundings, agreement on minimum budgetary levels
for spare parts and track maintenance is being sought under the project in
order to minimize the impact of budgetary constraints upon basic maintenance
requirements.
4.40      One uncertainty is the extent to which the Government will continue
acting on its policy of reducing subsidies and raising prices to reflect the
cost of services as political pressures mount. There is no effective way of
reducing this uncertainty. It should be noted, however, that the present
administrations of both the Government and N de M have clearly stated their
policy objectives, which are consistent with those of the project, and have
taken substantive actions since 1982 which confirm their commitment to those
policies.
V. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
5.01      At negotiations, agreement was reached on the following:
(a) that N de M will extend the economic evaluation guidelines to the
other railways' investment plans as they are incorporated within
N de M, and will maintain records with respect to project targets
which will exclude any newly incorporated railways (para 3.02);
(b) that tariffs for all rail commodities will be at least equal to
long-term variable costs by January 1988 (paras 3.14 and 4.20 (a));
(c) that by November of each year, beginning in 1985, N de M and SCT
will update the four-year investment program for Bank comment
(para 4.05);
(d) that detailed costing of passenger services will be carried out by
November 1986 (para 4.20 (b));



- 29 -
(e) that modern accounting and management information systems will be
installed by December 1987 (para 4.20 (c));
(f) that passenger fares will be raised to long-term variable costs by
January 1988 (para 4.20 (d));
(g) that the Government will provide compensation to N de M in a timely
manner for losses related to passenger services (para 4.20 Ce));
(h) that N de M will establish an accounting mechanism to monitor
workshop costs by September 1987 (para 4.21 (a));
(i) that N de M will carry out specific costing exercises for important
product movements by March 1986 (para 4.21 (b));
(j) that N de M will study organization and staffing of its commercial
department by September 1986 (para 4.21 (c));
(k) that N de M will prepare a marketing strategy by April 1987
(para 4.21 (d));
(1) that sufficient budgetary allocations will be provided for
locomotive maintenance (para 4.22 (a));
(m) that an agreed locomotive scrapping program will be executed
(para 4.22 (b));
(n) that the OCS will be fully installed by June 1986 (para 4.22 (c));
(a) that the cost and productivity of rehabilitated locomotives will be
monitored (para 4.22 (d));
(p) that sufficient budgetary allocations will be provided annually for
track maintenance (para 4.23 (a));
(q) that N de M will monitor and report annually on the
productivity of mechanized maintenance units (para 4.23 (b));
(r) that N de M will install and train on a Train Performance
Calculator by June 1987 (para 4.24 (a));
(s) that N de M will carry out a master plan study for future yards by
September 1986 (para 4.24 (b));
(t) that N de m -will carry out a middle-management training program
(para 4.25);
(u) that the subiect of revised passenger-coach designs will be
reviewed with the Bank by December 31, 1985 and that N de M will
establish a system for monitoring the costs of passenger coaches by
that date (para 4.36); and
(v) that SCT will complete all investments (para 4.37).



- 30 -
5.02      At negotiations, agreement on the following was confirmed:
(a) railway targets (para 4.26);
(b) procurement procedures and guidelines (para 4.35);
(c) disbursement procedures (para 4.37);
(d) auditing arrangements (para 4.38); and
(e) project reporting requirements (4.38).
5.03       A condition for loan effectiveness would be that the Government and
N de M have furnislied a plan of action satisfactory to the Bank for the
tariff reclassification of commodities and have taken initial action to
reclassify some of the commodities still carried at tariffs below long-term
variable costs (para 3.14).
5.04      Subject to the above assurances and conditions, the proposed
project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$300.0 million, with a term of
15 years, including a grace period of three years.
