- 57 -                             ANNEX 1
MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
Major Sectoral Issues
Scope and Timing of New Transport Investment
1.        The main issue in the transport sector is the appropriate scope and
timing of transport investments. Indications are that traffic is recovering
from the declines registered in 1982. The most impressive recovery has been
on the railways. Freight on Ferrocarriles Nacionales de Mexico (N de M) grew
by 13% in 1983 and by about 3% in 1984. Slower recovery has been estimated
for the other modes. Investment, however, was held down in 1983 and 1984
(about US$1.2-1.5 billion equivalent per year), and no major increases are
expected in 1985 as the Government continues to keep the lid on the budget.
Although there are a number of major new investments which the Government
wishes to implement, limited budget allocations have restricted such
projects, and SCT continues to focus primarily on maintenance and
reconstruction and on the completion of major ongoing works, such as the
doubling and electrification of the railway line between Mexico and Queretaro
and the new line between Guadalajara and Monterrey, the Lazaro Cardenas
industrial port and the Coatzacoalcos and Tampico bridges. Major new
investment outlays being considered are priority toll road widenings, the
coastal rail line between Tampico and Veracruz, continuing works at the
Altamira industrial port and at Manzanillo, extending railway electrification
from Queretaro to Irapuato and providing new airport capacity for Mexico
City. Other investments being studied are a suburban rail link between
Toluca and Mexico City and the northern road bypass, providing direct access
between northern Mexican cities and those to the east and south of Mexico
City. Most of these decisions will depend upon the expected timing and
structure of the economic recovery, the projected level of import
substitution and its impact upon grain imports and related transport
facilities, the transport requirements of non-traditional exports, and the
progress of industrial decentralization and urban deconcentration efforts.
The Bank's lending and sector work programs are designed to help the
Government to formulate an appropriate response to transport demand through
improved pricing policies and operational efficiency as well as investment
planning.
Fuel Pricing
2.        A Bank-financed road user charges study in 1975 found that refined
petroleum products were being priced at the pump well below world market
prices. This situation worsened as domestic inflation surged and gasoline
and diesel prices remained no higher than US$0.42 and US$0.18 per gallon
respectively during 1981. Since December 1981, however, the Government has
followed a policy of gradually raising domestic fuel prices toward
international levels (Table 1 of this annex). From December 1981 to January



- 58 -                               ANNEX 1
1985, gasoline prices rose from Mex$ 7/liter to Mex$ 70/liter
(US$1.38/gallon) for extra and Mex$ 2.8/liter to Mex$ 55/liter
(US$1.08/gallon) for regular, a real increase of 76% and 247% respectively.
Diesel prices were raised even more significantly from Mex$ 1.17/liter to
Mex$ 31.2/liter (US$0.61/gallon) for a real increase of over 367%. While
gasoline prices are above international levels (US$0.79/gallon), diesel
prices are about 77% of international price levels of US$0.79/gallon. 1/ In
January, the Government approved a monthly increase in diesel prices of 2.5%
to the end of this year, which, if coupled with additional periodic
increases, should gradually raise diesel prices in real terms.
3.         Action taken since the Second Highway Sector Loan (2428-ME) was
presented to the Board in May 1984 show that the Government continues to be
committed to resolving the fuel pricing issue and is trying to reduce the
price differential between diesel and gasoline while approaching
international levels. This policy has been confirmed by officials of SHCP
during the semi-annual discussions with the Bank. Understandably, political
concerns have determined the timing of the price increases. With the present
level of prices, no major distortions are evident in the transport
sector 2/. However, it is important that the Government not lose ground in
its efforts to reach international price levels, primarily to ensure pricing
equity among transport modes and among the various vehicle operators on the
nation's roads. The road user charges study (paras 4-6) is expected to focus
on the underpricing of diesel-operated vehicles on the roads and to propose
appropriate tax changes in this respect.
Road User Charges
4.         Related to the issue of fuel prices is the question of whether the
various road-related tolls and taxes imposed on road users sufficiently cover
the cost of providing road infrastructure. The general principle to be
applied in determining a minimum equitable distribution of user charges
should be that users pay the full costs of owning and operating the vehicle
and the attributable costs of operating, maintaining and reconstructing the
roadway, which vary in accordance with the number and weight of the various
types of vehicles. The charges which enter into this calculation in Mexico
include road and bridge tolls, fees for licensing, permits and public
transport concessions, producer taxes for tires and vehicles (over and above
normal taxation level), special import duties on vehicles and special taxes
on fuel. None of these charges are directly assigned to roads or identified
as road user charges, and all enter the federal treasury to be distributed in
accordance with general budgetary practices. Even road and bridge tolls
collected by the Toll Road and Bridge Authority, Caminos y Puentos Federales
(CyPF), are def±ned as being a tax and are turned over to the federal
treasury.
5.        With the surge in inflation, the subsequent devaluations and price
changes, an updated analysis of road user charges is required. Prior studies
and Bank estimates showed that the gross receipts from user taxes covered all
1/ Based on mid-November 1984 Caribbean market prices, FOB.
2/ See Annex 1 in Staff Appraisal Report for Second Highway Sector Project
(Loan 2428-ME), April 1984.



- 59 -                              ANNEX 1
administrative, construction, reconstruction and maintenance costs for the
federal, state and local networks. However, fuel tax revenues represented
the major contribution in these analyses, based on the fact that special
taxes on fuel reprepent about 45% of the total pump price. Since pump prices
are generally at or below international levels or their opportunity costs,
fuel tax revenues, in economic terms, cannot be considered as a contribution
to road infrastructure. Without this element, the results of the prior
analyses would be negative, with economic road user charges covering little
more than maintenance and administration. The deficit is apparently not
equally distributed over the network. A Bank-sponsored sector study of the
Mexican toll roads found that, with the increases in tolls along the 1,000-km
toll road network of almost 500% in the past year, gross toll revenues (about
US$135 million) should have been sufficient, in 1984, to cover all
administrative, operating and maintenance costs of CyPF and all debt service,
as well as to contribute to capital extensions and widenings.
6.        As fuel prices near international levels, it is time for the
Government to attempt to resolve the road user charges issue. A road user
charges study has been initiated which will focus on the overall balance
between tax receipts and road expenditures and on the allocation of taxes and
costs by type of interurban road user. Prior analyses concluded that
diesel-powered commercial vehicle operators, particularly for heavy trucks,
were paying a lower percentage of the costs that they impose upon the network
than were other types of road users. The study is expected to recommend
additional taxes to resolve observed deficiencies. SCT will complete the
study by December 1985 and will subsequently discuss with the Bank
alternative policy options and timing for resolving identified deficiencies.
Railway Financial Status
7.        The issues relating to the railways' financial status are discussed
in detail in Annex 2 and, thus, are not treated here.
Port Charges
8.        Port dues, levied on ships, and wharfage charges, levied on cargo -
intended to cover the capital costs of port infrastructure, its maintenance
and administration - long ago ceased to bear any relation to such costs.
Even though the present Government drastically increased port dues in 1983,
revenues from both port dues and wharfage were estimated to cover no more
than 22% of annual depreciation of the current value of SCT's port assets.
The Lazaro Cardenas Industrial Port Project (Loan 2450-ME), therefore,
required that SCT complete a study of port dues by September 1985 and an
action plan for the progressive application of cost-related port charges.
9.        Port charges for services given to ships and cargr by the Port
Operating Companies (ESPs) have generally been adjusted to keep pace with
inflation. The SCT's Directorate General for Operations has declared its
commitment to keeping such operating companies financially self-sufficient.
Specifically for Lazaro Cardenas, a cost and tariff study of port services
has been undertaken, and it is intended to serve as a mDdel for such studies
throughout the port subsector.



- 60 -                              ANNEX 1
.Rural Road Maintenance
10.       Rural road maintenance is lacking nationwide.  Since 1971, the
Government's attention has been directed at the construction of rural roads
with minimum design standards. With over 80,000 km built in ten years, the
requirements for maintenance, particularly periodic maintenance including
spot improvements, are accumulating rapidly. Rural road inventory procedures
have been inappropriate for identifying maintenance requirements, and it is
clear, from limited field inspections, that the need is significant and that
the US$300/km allocated in 1983 hardly met 50% of the needs. In the state of
Chiapas, estimates were made on the cost of routine and periodic maintenance
which showed that about US$800/km is required, of which about 552 represents
periodic and emergency maintenance. Taking into account the deferred
maintenance works, it is expected that the budgetary requirements in the
coming years may be even higher in several states. Under the Second Highway
Sector Project (Loan 2428-ME), the Government agreed to provide adequate
resources to maintain rural roads appropriately. Already, in 1984, the
Government increased the rural roads maintenance budget by over 45Z in real
terms and has projected substantial increases in future years by allowing
rural road construction funds to be use' for reconstruction purposes as
well. To assist ir defining the specific level of resources required, the
Government has initiated a rural roads inventory which is expected to cover
100% of the network by June 1987, applying agreed upon inventory procedures.
The Government will develop a program of periodic maintenance works from the
inventory data and will review progress with the Bank during the semi-annual
meetings through supervision of the highway loan.

MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
N de M Financial Status and Forecast Details
A. Financial Performance to Date
(i) Operating Results
1.        Despite the steady growth in traffic over the years of the Third
Railway Project (1975-1981) (1.7% annually in tons, and 4.7% in ton-km), the
gap between N de M's revenue and costs widened considerably. In real terms,
revenues increased by 6.3% over this period, while costs rose by 20.3%, with
the operating loss growing by 80%. Only after the crisis of 1982, reduced
traffic, and the coming to power of a new Government administration were
serious steps taken to reverse this trend. Tariffs were increased in early
June and September by 50% and 40% (freight), and 25Z and 40% (passengers)
respectively, following on the 35% (freight and passenger) tariff increase or
mid-December 1982, plus a 4.5% passenger fare increase on January 6, 1983.
The combined effect of these increases, together with the increase in traffic
of 1983 (some 12.9% in ton-km freight and 20% in passenger-km over 1982
figures) 1, was a 28% real increase in N de W's revenues for that year as
compared to 1982.   Higher utilization of capacity and more efficient
operations management, while costs were kept strictly under control, resulted
in lower real working costs and the first working surplus ever in the
railway's recent history.
2.                Table 1 of this annex gives audited revenue statements for
the years 1980-1983 in current pesos, and an estimate for 1984. Because of
the impact on costs of inflation and of currency devaluation in Mexico, and
of tariff increases on railway revenues, it is difficult to compare actual
figures with those forecast at the beginning of the Fourth Railway Project
(Loan 1929-ME). However, a review of the targeted and actual financial
ratios shows that there has been solid improvement. The working and
operating ratios improved from 1.38 and 1.60 respectively in 1980, to 0.85
and 1.08 in 1983, which was better than the Loan 1929-ME appraisal forecast.
3.        For comparison purposes, the annual revenue and expenditure figures
of Table 1 have been deflated to 1980 values, using domestic consumer price
indices, and are given on the next page in summarized form (details in
Table 2 of this annex).

4.        Table 2 indicates that, while railway operating revenues increased
slightly in 1981 in real terms, they fell sharply in 1982, despite the four
tariff increases of that year, but increased to the 1980 level again in 1983,
with the aid of further tariff increases which exceeded the inflation rate
(Table 3.4 of the text). The improvement in N de M's financial performance
has come about largely as a result of reduced operating costs. The better
usage of the railways' capacity (illustrated by the figures on page 2 of
Table 3.2) clearly indicates N de M's more efficient control and management
of its resources. In addition, the Government's policy of restricting wage
increases resulted in railway labor costs declining substantially, in real
terms, since 1981. M4aterials (including fuel) costs have also declined in
real terms since 1980, probably because of lower consumption of spares for
locomotives and rolling stock, and also because of reduced traffic in 1982
and financial stringencv in other years. Net expenditure on car rentals had
become a net revenue item by 1983 because of increased efficiency in the use
of rolling stock (see Table 3.2, page 2 - Operational Efficiencies).
5.       Against these reductions in operating costs, financial costs have
risen sharply, largely because of the continual devaluation of the Mexican
peso. In 1983, loan interest absorbed 46% of operating revenues, compared
with only 21% in 1980. Total debt service in 1983 amounted to MexS 37
billion in current pesos (about USS250 million eauivalent).
6.       Although annual depreciation was nominally increased from MexS 2.9
billion in 1.980 to MexS 14.3 billion in 1983, the annual amounts declined,
in constant pesos, in 1981 and 1982, but, after the fixed assets revaluation
of 1983, rose again above the 1980 level. Annual revaluation of assets is
mandatory when annual inflation exceeds 50%.

-65 -                               ANNEX 2
calculates the long-term variable costs of freight commodities and of
individual passenger services. Long-term variable costs (LTVC) are
distinguished from short-term ones (STVC) by the inclusion of depreciation
and interest (real) on assets used, in calculating the unit costs.
11.      Cost data prepared by N de M for the various main freight traffics,
using actual costs to date, revised to reflect the inflation expected in 1984
and compared with revenues, taking into account the latest tariff increase,
show that, for about 62% of the traffic (in terms of tonnage), revenues now
cover at least their respective LTVCs. N de M's cost calculations
consistently show that, for freight traffic, such variable costs amount to
77% of fully distributed costs. These cost and revenue data, which cover a
broad sample (82%) of the estimated 1984 freight tonnage, indicate that
total freight revenues would be Mex$ 121 billion as against total variable
costs of Mex$ 112 billion, giving a contribution of Mex$ 9 billion toward
fixed costs.
12.       The revenues for certain major traffics (including iron ore,
cement, coal, coke, sugar cane, dolomite and lime), totaling about 23.6
million tons (about 38% of the 1984 estimated total), will fail, however, to
cover their estimated LTVCs. The tariffs charged for these commodities
lie mostly within the lower three or four of the 20 classes of N de M's
tariff discussed in the following paragraphs. Table 3.6 of the text gives,
for 24 main commodities, data concerning tariff class, average haul, and
estimates, for 1984, of tonnages, variable costs and revenues, together with
ratios of the percentage coverage of revenues over costs, of the degree to
which highway truck tariffs exceed those of the railway, and of the
percentage which the average rail cost bears to the product selling price.
For the majority of the rail freight traffics listed in this table, the rail
cost element of total selling price is well under 10% - thus, even increases
of 50% or more in the rail tariff would not mater'ally affect delivered
costs. However, in the case of thr-ee of the major traffics (coal, dolomite
and gypsum), the table indicates rail cost percentages of 23.0, 18.3 and 36.4
respectively. Coal and dolomite are low rated commodities, the revenues from
which do not cover variable costs, and for which the Government has been
reluctant to allow N de M to raise tariffs substantially.
13.       Passenger fares are calculated at a flat rate per km of travel.  As
of September 1, 1984, the rates were: 2/

First Class - Sleeping Car           MexS 1.801 per pass-km
First Class - Special Car             Mex$ 1.801 per pass-km
First Class - Regular Car            Mex$ 1.466 per pass-km
Second Class                         Mex$ 0.877 per pass-km
Rail Cars                            Mex$ 1.801 per pass-km
There are separate charges for special facilities, such as berths in sleeping
cars.  These rates per pass-km are extremely low, at about, or less than, one
U.S. cent per pass-km. It is obvious that such low fares are wholly

N de M.



- 66 -                              ANNEX 2
insufficient to pay for the services provided.  In 1983, the direct costs
(including depreciation and interest) for passenger services amounted to
Mex$ 12.8 billion (including express parcel service and mail), against which
revenues of only about Mex$ 4.2 billion were received, making a loss of
Mex$ 8.6 billion.
14.       Reducing the number of classes, particularly at the lower end,
would simplify N de M's tariff, and, by shifting lower rated commodities into
higher classes, would automatically generate u.z-p revenues.  SCT, however, is
not disposed to change the present rigid classifiz'ation, although it agrees,
in principle, to move those items not covering LTV'C to a higher class and to
introdtuce some commercial flexibility within controlled bounds. Indeed, to
be able to use such commercial flexibility, the present costing done by
N de M would need considerable refinement. This would be part of the
technical assistance rendered under the proposed loan.
C. Principal Financial Issues
i5.       The preceding discussion has indicated that, while N de M's
financial performance has improved over the last two years, several problem
areas remain to be solved before a satisfactory situation is achieved. These
are discussed in the paragraphs following.
(i) Freight Tariffs
16.       Revenues from freight and passenger tariffs have been insufficient
to meet working expenses (except in 1983), depreciation and interest, let
alone provide reserves for developnent. Furthermore, the structure of
freight tariffs contains several distortions, such that, for several
important commodities, the revenues fali tu cover even the related long-term
variable costs. The problem of dealing with tariffs is compounded by the
continued inflation in Mexico. This has generally resulted in corrective
tariff action being too late, and leads to reluctance on the part of the
Government to take effective rail tariff action, for fear of adding .o
inflationary pressure.   Similarly, with regard to specific tariff increases,
major commodities incurring the highest deficits are often low priced
products for which transport costs represent a high proportion of the
delivered price, making specific increases politically sensitive. Thus, even
though the Government has agreed that restructuring of N de M's tariff is
necessarv no action has yet been taken.
(ii) Passenger services
17.       Revenues from passenger fares are well below their short run
variable costs. The Government is reluctant to raise rares sufficently to
cover costs, particularly since the rolling stock is old, and the services
provided are generally poor. Even when freight tariffs are raised, passenger
fares are often increased at a lower rate, if at all. In conjunction with
the Fourth Railway Project, the Government agreed to reimburse N de M for the
losses incurred by operating passenger services. In addition, N de M has
recently concluded a comprehensive study of passenger ser.ices; its
conclusions and recommendations are discussed in Annex 6. A progressive
reduction of all passenger service subsidies is proposed such that all
services reach LTVC by January 1988, except for certain second-class
passenger services for which fares may be set below LTVC because of social
and economic consideratIons.



- 67 -                               AN NEX 2
(iii) Debt Burden
18.       Servicing of N de M's debt has become an increasing problem for the
railway, which so far has been unable to contribute, from its own resources,
any funds for this purpose. A considerable proportion of this debt,
estimated at about 40%, was incurred to finance operating losses, or related
debt service, and was the result of the Government's refusal to allow timely
tariff adjustments when costs were rising steeply.
19.       In order to limit the debt burden to manageable proportions,
related purely to capital investments, enab'iL"tg LthL railway to reach
financial autonomy by 1988, the Government would need to assume no less than
40% of N de M's long-term debt.
D. Financial Projections
(i)   Assumptions Used
20.       The financial projections presented in this report are made in 1984
pesos or dollars. For revenue projections, the low traffic forecasts
(Annex 3) have been used, with freight increasing at a rate of 3% per year.
These traffic forecasts have been converted to revenues using (a) the revised
freight tariff of September 1, 1984 and (b) the revised passenger fares of
March 1, 1984. In addition, projected freight revenues have been increased
annually by a conservative 2% per year, in real terms, although Government
officials project 3-5% per year tariff increase. The reclassification of
certain cargoes, now being carried at rates lower than variable cost, is
taken into account, and it is assumed that some action on this matter will be
taken by spring of 1985. This last item is estimated to add approximately 5%
to the annual freight revenue base. For passenger traffic revenues, the
presently agreed upon LTVC's specific passenger subsidy, to make up the
approximately 40% of costs not covered by fares, has been included in the
revenue projections, even though Government commitment will be sought to
progressively raise fares and reduce subsidies.
21.       On the cost side, wages and salaries have been forecast to take
into account:
(a) the wage awards of 1984;
(b) the slight increase in manpower forecast over the project period
(about 3.4% overall); and
(c) the effect of incentive pay, which forms part of the last labor
agreement and of the Modernization Plan for the Railways. Since
precise details are not yet available on the manner in which the
incentive pay will be calculated, reserves have been built into the
forecasts.
A further assumption is that wages and salaries will be held at this real
level in the face of inflation throughout the project period.
22.        It is assumed that pensions, due to those pensioners not yet
covered by the Social Security Institute (IMSS), will continue to be paid
directly by the railway; the cost has been removed from the railway
operational expenses and classified as non-operating expenses. The forecasts



- 68 -                              ANNEX 2
also take into account reimbursement of these costs by the Government, as
confirmed by Government officials. Materials cost (other than fuel) is
assumed to increase in accordance with traffic forecast increases. For fuel
and lubricants costs, the consumption is based on freight and passenger
locomotive-km, which, in turn, are derived from the forecasts of freight
ton-km and passenger-km, using average past indices. Consumption in liters
is costed at the current 1984 price. Net car rentals are expected to become
positive in line with freight traffic and better utilization of rolling
stock.
23.       Depreciation is calculated at an average annual rate of 2.5% on the
gross fixed-assets value as of the end of the previous financial year, which
is the weighted average of different categories of structures and equipment.
24.       Forecast loan interest charges take into account present long-term
debt and the effect of assumed new debt (proposed new IBRD loan, Exim-Bank
and other commercial lenders).  In addition, 36% has been added as the
estimated average effect of peso devaluation in 1984. For long-term debt,
the Government's agreement to assume a portion of N de M's existing debt over
three years is assumed to be initiated in 1985. The 46% assumed is
equivalent to the total of former loans incurred by N de M to cover
operational losses when timely Government agreement on needed tariff
increases was not forthcoming, and the amounts of Government contributions
were insufficient to cover resulting operational deficits.  The estimated
peso devaluation during 1984 is also taken into account. This form,er
long-term debt becomes Government equity in N de M.

26.       The forecast financial situation indicates a considerable
improvement in N de M's financial performance. While the railway has made
significant progress to date, particularly in unit cost reductions, largely
by carrying more traffic with only minor increases in its labor force,
further action is required to enhance revenues, reduce losses caused by
passenger traffic, and relieve N de M of some of its debt burden. The
actions which form the basis of the financial projections imply a continued
commitment by the Government to its stated policy of putting the railway on a
sound financial basis.
27.       The forecast funds-flow shows that, although N de M's revenues
will, in each year, be more than adequate to cover working expenses, the net
operating revenues, because of the impact of depreciation, will not be
sufficient to cover loan interest. However, by 1988, its cash generation
will be adequate to service both interest and loan repayments, and a modest
return on the current value of net fixed assets will be earned. Forecast
cash flows are shown in Table 5 of this annex.
28.       The table shows that, even with N de M's improved earnings and ca,h
generation, considerable sums will still be needed annually from the
Government, ranging from US$337 million equivalent in 1985 to US$460 million
equivalent in 1987. Despite the Government's assumption of some of the debt,
N de M's remaining debt service obligation is considerable - on the order of
US$240-300 million equivalent annually.
29.       Investment, in the years 1986-1988, includes assets financed by SCT
(largely track works) and handed over to N de M on completion. The actual
investment by SCT during the project period is much higher, as noted in
Annex 5.

- 76 -                              ANNEX 3
MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
Basis of Freight Traffic Forecasts
A. The Economy and Railway Traffic Growth
1.   For the three decades preceding the mid-1970s, Mexico was very
successful in achieving rapid economic growth while also maintaining
stability in prices and the balance of payments. From 1940 to 1970, Gross
Domestic Product (GDP) growth exceeded 6% in real terms, inflation averaged
less chan 5% per year from the mid-1960s to 1972, and the dollar value of the
peso, fixed in 1954, was maintained until 1976. During the mid-_970s,
however, Mexico experienced increasing public sector deficits, inflation,
large balance-of-payments deficits, capital flight and a marked slowdown in
the real rate of GDP growth, dropping to less than 3.57 in 1Q77. Ecnnomic
stabilization measures, followed by major oil discoveries, refueled the
Mexican economy, and GDP rose by 8-9% from 1978 to 1981.  This suirge was
short-lived, however, as the oil market slackened, interest rates rose on the
debt acquired to support increasing investment, an appreciating peso Proded
the competitiveness of domestic tradeable sectors and private capital fled.
GDP dropped by 0.5% in 1982 and hy 5.3% in 1983.
2.   With the comprehensive stabilization program oIf the new administration
which took office in December 1982, the prospects for economic recovery are
good. In 1984, GDP is expected to have risen by 3.5%, and a growth of 4.5%
is projected for 1985, reaching 5.5-6% by 1988. The forecast depends upon a
broadening of Mexico's trade base beyond oil, the development of
non-traditional exports and a rationalization of public sector pricing and
investment policies.
3.   Transport has always been a key element in the successful development of
Mexico, and the railways, despite their low share in overall traffic (14% of
all ton-km or 23% of road and -a-l ton-km), are crucial to the efficient
functioning of several sectors. The railways carry about 40% of all heans,
about 30% of all sorghum, about 50% of all wheat and ahout 10% of all corn
p:oduced and imported by Mexico. The railways are also the principal carrier
for iron ore, coke, coal and sugar and are a maior carrier of fertilizers,
cement and many steel products. The importance of the railways was
underscored in 1978-1979 when the country's surge in econnmic growth and a
record import of grain due to a poor local harvest put a severe strain upon
existing infrastructure capacitv. Inadequate railwav services affected the
movement of basic grains, iron ore and pellets for the steel industry, raw
materials and finished products for the fertilizer industry and industrial
imports to serve oil expansion by PEMEX. Furthermore, the railways are
considered essential to new industrial development in areas outside Mexico
City, the industrial ports of Lazaro Cardenas and Altamira.
4.   Railway traffic has generally followed the growth in GDP.  From 1970 to
1984, both GDF and railway freight ton-km increased hy an average annual rate
of 5.1%. N de M's freight traffic grew from 3q.1 million tons and 18.4
billion ton-kin in 1970 to an estimated 61.9 million toT;s and 37.1 billion



- 77 -                              ANNEX 3
ton-km by 1984, with ton-km growing faster than tons (5.1% compared wit'
3.3%) as average hauls increased from 470 to about 600 km per ton during the
1970-1984 period (Table 3.3 of the text). In contrast to the worldwide
trend, Mexico's railway traffic has increased steadily, and railway's share
in the transport of its principal commodities has not changed significantly
except in the case of petroleum and derivatives, for which pipelines have
replaced rail se-vices. Excluding petroleum products, the historic annual
growth for N de M tons and ton-km from 1970 to 1984 is 3.7% and 5.7%
respectively.
5.   The most important group of commodities, in terms of ton-km, carried by
N de M consists of intermediate industrial products (cements, fertilizers,
sugar, petrochemicals and steel) representing 32% of total ton-km in 1984,
mineral products representing 27% and agricultural products 25%. This
distribution is similar to the composition of traffic in 1970, with the
reduction in petroleum products being distributed proportionately. The main
commodities, in order of ton-km in 1984, are iron ore (17%), fertilizers
(6%), sorghum (6%), cement (6%), corn (5%), wheat (4%), fuel oil (4%) and
coal (4%).
6.   Traffic mainly follows a radial pattern with Mexico City as the focal
point. The main corridors are Mexico-Queretaro-Guadalajara-Manzanillo;
Mexico-Queretaro-Monterrey-Nuevo Laredo; Mexico-Queretaro-Torreon-Ciudad
Juarez; and Mexico-Veracruz-Coatzacoalcos. There are also a growing number
of traffic movements between secondary cities such as Guadalajara and
Monterrey which require roundabout routings because of the radial pattern of
the network. The heaviest traffic flow (freight) is between Mexico City,
Queretaro, Irapuato and Guadalajara, ranging from over 8.5 to 14.5 million
net tons per year. North of Queretaro to Monterrey and Nuevo Laredo at the
U.S. border, traffic flows range from over 3 million to over 8 million net
tons annually. The other major cargo flow from Mexico City toward Veracruz
and Coatzacoalcos ranges from over 3 million to 4.5 million net tons per
year.
B. Forecast Methodology
7.   Previous traffic forecasts prepared by N de M in conjunction with Bank
projects have proven to be over-optimistic. The Third Railway Project
(Loan 1232-ME) estimated an annual growth rate of 7.8% for ton-km from 1974
to 1980, compared with an actual rate of 4.6% during that period. Similarly,
under the Fourth Railway Project (Loan 1929-ME), the forecast for 1979-1984
was an increase of 7.6% in ton-km per year, compared with the actual rate of
3.8%. Two factors make railway freight forecasting ditficult in Mexico. The
first is the volatility of the Mexican economy. Both the Third and Fourth
Railway Projects covered periods of severe economic recession in 1976 and
1982. The second factor is the uncertainty regarding grain imports. The
railways carry a high percentage of gra'n imports so that, in years of poor
harvests, rail tonnage increases while, in years of good harvests, rail
tonnage declines. Mexico thus revises its forecasts every year and adjusts
its investment plans, particularly for locomotives and rolling stock,
accordingly.



- 78 -                               ANNEX 3
8.   To minimize the problem of tincertainty, the proposed project institutes
the following with regard to rall traffie forecasts:
(a)  forecasting methodology and data hase have br-en upgraded,
particularlv for grain;
(b)  an annual review process Is heing I nstitittlonal ized; and
(c)  two forecasts have ben prepared:  :i commervial forecast following
N de M's detailed prodLct-by-prodict met1thodology and a low forecast
based on the hi storical tendency i,f the last 14 ye.ars.
The commercial forecast has been appli ed to determinen Investment requirements
to ensure that the railways h.vi' s;ufficient caparcitv to serve thie identifIed
market.  The low forecast has bten used in the financial projection to ensure
that N de M will be ahle to maintain an adtqritiate financial position even if
investments are wade and expected tra"1< f1 c es nct m:iterialIze.
9.   The traffic forecasts prepared hy N' dc M andl revlivwed by the Bank were
based on a product-by-prodtuct ;an.alvsis.  T!ho -level ol' detail applied varied
by type of product, depending on itS Importanct to overall  tr.affic, degree of
"eaptiveness" and commercial possibilities  tor significant change In N de M's
market share.  For major produicts, special re-ports were prepared hased on
discussions with affected industrivs on their expected transport needs.  For
other commodities, appropriate statistical relationships were developed with
population, GDP and other variables.  For grain products, a consultant study
was commissioned.  All ra,I traffic -was ass!gied to the lines related to
projected origins and destinations for  izijor commoditles or In accordance
with the present line distribution for other prdchic-ts.
10.  The commercial forecast for 10.84-]989 predicts .an ;nrc-0  lricrease in
rail tonnage of 5.3% and in ton-kmn of 5.O!'-, rising from 61.9 to 76.2 millIon
tons and 37.1 to 45.2 billion ton-km (Trnblv L i of tibe text).  The distance
per ton Is expected  to stabilizv at ahmtw  'Al3 km.  Major traffic Increases
are expected for certain grains and intermed':te ind'ivtrial preducts for
which N de M believes it can attaIn a highir rmark'et. *h:hre and Is targeting
special commercial efforts.  Tht *iow" forecast whlcbh  .  ipplied in the
financial evaluation assumes an alnnu.il growth r-;- _! f aboui 3.0O/ for tonnage,

historical trend over the ls.t l*4 v':irs.
C. Agricultural Products
11.  N de M's agricultural trafflc is pro fecte-d- to grow hy about 5.27
annuallv.  A consultant study, commissioned h,v N de M. projected national
consumption, domestic production and req1irot; importatior. for basic oralns.
This forecast was used to develop a table- of oriylns ^r.nd dsstirations to
determine transport requirements. Roaid and r.ii' traffic were analyzed to
assess each mode's comparative advantage, .nnL .i"1al assignment was made
based on cost, current shipper preference .n_ th--  ailway's assessment of
potential increases in market share.  Pnlv m!74al traffic increases are
expected for corn and wheat as internal production improves, :es.sening
imports and, thus, rail demand.  The most signific-int increases are expected
for sorghum.  With the concentration cf douestic production ix. northeastern



- 79 -                              ANNEX 3
Mexico, sorghum is a prime candidate for increasing railways' modal share. In
recent years, railway sorghum traffic has increased well above the average
for other commodities, and a newly consolidated shipping office for CONASUPO,
the national grain marketing company, and the continued favorable tariff
differential should assure this market for N de M.
D. Miueral Products
12. Mineral traffic is expected to grow by about 2.7% per year. Iron ore,
which represents over half of this traffic, is the major element in this
below-average growth because a slurry pipeline of 380 km, constructed by
SIDERMEX, will divert traffic away from N de M to the AHMSA Monclova and
Fundidora Monterrey steel plants. Also, any new steel Dlant capacity
expansions are expected to be located near ports and served by imported iron
ore such as the Altamira projects of SIDERMEX and HYLSA. For existing
movements, an analysis was made of iron ore reserves and possible changes in
traffic patterns.
13. Coal traffic was analyzed in a manner similar to that used for iron ore
and is expected to grow by 4.2% annually. The main growth in demand is to
serve the AIMSA steel. plants at Monclova, reaching almost 3 million tons per
year by 1983. Some traffic growth is also expected to the SICARTSA plant at
Lazaro Carde.-as by 1986.
E. Petroleum and Derived Products
14. Although petroleum and related products traditionally have left the
railways ir favor of pipelines, there have been increasing movements of fuel
oil carried in PEMEX-owned tank cars, increasing by 12.3% per year from 1980
to 1984. N de M planners are cautious about this traffic and its potential
and have projected a 4.1% annual increase to 1988 based on forecast fuel oil
production.
F. Inorganic Products
15. Limestone represents 36% of railway tonnage in inorganic products; it is
expected to grow from 2.8 million tons in 1984 to 3.6 million tons in 1986
and then remain constant. The underlving assumption is that new cement
plants will locate near their primary product sources and that railway
traffic growth will be limited to the expansion of existing plants,
especially at Tlanepantla.
G. Industrial Products
16. The category of industrial products covers a wide variety of commodities
and is the area ii. which N de M expects to focus more intensive marketing
efforts during the next four years. Overall growth is expected to be 6.9%
per year. Sugar traffic, which represents 10% of the product tonnage in this
category, is expected to grow by 6.2% per year, assuming that N de M's share
of national production will stay constant at about 55%. Similarly, cement
traffic, about 34% of N de M's industrial products tonnage, is expected to
grow by 6.5% per year as the railways maintain about a 30% share of total
production, taking new plants into account. In 1984, cement traffic rose by
26% as the railways carried new cement exports, an indication of the
railwayst role in the movemei.t of non-traditional exports.



- 80 -                              ANNEX 3
17. After the decline in fertilizer traffic experienced in 1980-1981 because
of the capacity limitations of N de M. the railways have regained much of
this traffic, growing from 1.i million tLns tc 3.3 million tons from 1981 to
1984. N de M has targeted fertilizers as having major growth potential and
increasing railways' share, reaching 5.6 million tons by 1988 and growing
from 54% of national consumption in 1984 to 78% in 1988 (compared with 90% of
earlier years). A product-by-product analysis was conducted, and assumptions
about new plari-s ar'. pipeline connections were made.
18. N de M ha. also targeted finished steel products (pipes, construction
bars and plates, etc.) as potential new railway markets. In 1984,
significant traffic increases had already been realized. However, marketing
efforts are not expected to have substantaiL results before 1988.
H. New Types of Traffic
19. N de M is reviewing new types of services, including trailer and
container-on flat cars, consolidation centers, special trains for smaller
loads on high density lines and special unit trains such as a new daily
automobile train between the U.S. and Mexico City. These services will have
to be costed and new marketing approaches designed before such traffic can be
projected.

MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
N de M Investment Program 1985-1988
A. Composition of Investment Program
1.        The principal items in the N de M Investment Program 1985-88 are:
(a) Rehabilitation of Track
(b) Track Machinery
(c) Workshop Extensions and Improvements
(d) Extension of Radio Communications
(e) Extension of Centralized Traffic Control (CTC)
(f) New and Reconditioned Locomotives
(g) New and Reconditioned Freight Cars
(h) New Passenger Cars
B. Rehabilitation of Track
2.        N de M has prepared detailed proposals for work to be initiated in
1985, using new 115 lb/yd rail on main traffic lines and recovered lighter
weight rail, in suitable condition, on secondary lines. A note on the
specifications used is given in Attachment 1 of this annex. The
rehabilitation proposals, together with notes on the existing rail age and
condition, are given in Table 4.4 of the text. Rehabilitation programs have
been reduced, in recent years, to around 250 km per year because of budget
constraints; however, the deteriorating condition of much of the track listed
has been causing concern at N de M because of the increasing numbers of
accidents (Table 3.8 of the text) and delays from speed restrictions. A
special effort is therefore to be made in 1985 to carry out a larger program
totaling about 600 km (339 km with new rail and 259 km with recovered rail).
There will be a carryover, into 1986, of the rehabilitation of line G
(Cordoba - Medias Aguas), and, if the provision of track machinery is
delryed, the overall program could be delayed also. The programs will
cortinute with further works in each of the years 1986 to 1988. These later
programs have yet to be detailed by N de M, but are expected to average about
-I0 km of rehabilitation each year. At the completion of the 1988 program,
the total length of track laid in 115 lb/yd rail will be about 4,000 km (as
against 1,200 km in 1974), and about 74% of N de M's 15,480 km of track will
be provided with rail of 100 lb!yd weight or heavier.
C. Track Machinery
3.        N de H has experienced increasing difficulty over the past few
years to obtain, and retain, sufficient labor for manual track maintenance.
The heavy work involved is not popular, gang-strengths are low (four to seven
men, plus foreman, per 14 km lengths), and absenteeism is high. In acdition,
manual packing of track ballast, under the heavy rail and concrete sleepers
now used to carry increased locomotive and freight car axle loads, is not
effective in preserving line and level. Track maintenance standards have,



- 82 -                              ANNEX 4
as a result, deteriorated, and accidents due to track conditions have
increased; N de M has, therefore, decided to make a concentrated effort to
change from the present situation, in which about 90% of maintenance is by
hand and 10% by machine, to a position in which the percentages will become
25% and 75% respectively. In addition, much of the machinery obtained in
past years and used mostly on rehabilitation works is now over-aged and in
need of replacement and supplementing in certain areas. N de M's Way and
Works Department has, therefore, developed a list of machinery (Table 4.5 of
the text) which is based upon the concept of equipping seven main categories
of work, as shown in Attachment 2 of this annex.
4.        With the mechanized maintenance system, each through-packing group
wi]l be programed to do 1 km per day over 220 days per year, or a total of
about 2,800 km per year. N de M considers that about 50% of its system
length (or about 7,500 km) is suitable for machine ballast packing; the
rotation will vary considerably, depending upon the physical conditions and
the traffic level, and could be from one to four years. The spot packing
programs will be prepared by the Divisional engineers but will be coordinated
by the head office of the Way and Works Department. The existing labor will
be grouped into larger gangs of 22 men provided with gang trollies and hand-
held tools, each to cover about 50 km and to deal with the lighter
maintenance work adjacent to the track. The standards of maintenance
achieved can be assessed from time to time on the graphs produced by a track
condition recording unit already in use on the system.
5.        The list of equipment proposed for purchase in 1985 still leaves a
balance of some items to be obtained in subsequent years. Amounts are
allocated in the Investment Program for 1986-1988 to cover both the
additional equipment and the replacement of existing units now in poor
condition and not repairable. Budget provision has been made by N de M in
1985 for the additional machine operators and mechanics required; training is
being provided at the N de M training institute, and contracts for some of
the major new equipment will specify that the suppliers will also assist with
the training of operators and mechanics. Additionally, a new large, fully
equipped maintenance workshop is being constructed at Pantaco, in Mexico
City, and is due to be completed about June 1986.
D. Workshop Extensions and Improvements
6.        The maintenance of N de M locomotives is based upon:  (a) daily
inspections in service; (b) monthly inspections at depots; (c) half-yearly
repairs; and 'd) two-yearly and four-yearly general overhauls in main
workshops. However, the existing buildings and equipment are not adequate to
meet these programs. Three measures are proposed by the Department of
Locomotives and Rolling Stock: (a) a proper organization of the procedures
for the inspection of locomotives at depots; (b) the allocation to workshops
of adequate resources for the half-yearly repair cycle; and (c) the
enlargement of the principal workshops for general overhauls at San Luis
Potosi to serve all the fleet. The system of inspection and maintenance of
freight and passenger cars is also to be overhauled, and a system of car
repair depots (Spot-System) is to be established at strategic points to deal
with light repairs; workshops are to be provided to deal with medium and
heavy repairs.



- 83 -                              ANNEX 4
7.        Following a review of the facilities available, the Department has
drawn up two development proposals for 1985 investment, one for buildings and
facilities and depots and workshops, and the other for adequate machinery and
equipment. Budgetary constraints have led to some reduction in these
programs, and some items will have to be deferred to following years.
However, the programs are reasonably conceived and should enable N de M to
maintain its locomotive fleet to a better standard with higher percentage
availability.
E. Communications
8.        The original VHF/UHF communications system on N de M has been
completed, but the former FUS Railway, recently brought into N de M as the
Merida Division, still has to be incorporated into the N de M communications
system. This, however, will be carried out during the 1986-1988 period when
the remaining three separate railways - Pacifico, Chihuahua al Pacifico, and
Sonora-Baja California - will also be prepared for extensions to the
communications system.
9.        The main works proposed for 1985 are:
(a) acquisition and installation of radio communications for 700
locomotives;
(b) acquisition of portable VHF radios for track foremen, crews on
passenger and freight trains and some other special service
categories;
(c) extension of teleprinter facilities; and
Cd) enlargement of telephone exchange facilities at some stations,
stores, depots and workshops.
10.       The operational control system (SCINCO) has been largely brought
into use and has also been interconnected with that of the American
Association of Railways (AAR) in Washington. On N de M, 52 centers
throughout the system have been connected, leaving seven to be completed. In
most cases, only minor work is needed to enable these remaining centers to be
operational.
F. Signaling
11.       In pursuance of the policy of installation of Centralized Traffic
Control (CTC) on the busiest single-line sections of N de M, the main items
of work for 1985 are:
(a) completion of the installation of CTC between Guadalajara and
Irapuato (length 250 km); all material supplied, and it is
planned to complete the work in mid-1985;
(b) installation of CTC between San Luis Potosi and Benjamin
Mendez toward Saltillo - length 345 km; imported material on
order, and it is planned to obtain local material arnd install
during 1985; however, because of budgt.t reduction, the work
may carry over into 1986;



- 84 -                              ANNEX 4
(c) initial purchase of imported material for
Lecheria-Teotihuacan-Veracruz (length 412 kun); the detailed
study for this work has been completed recently; the purchase
of all material, imported and local, and the installation will
probably take two to three years, that is, into 1986 or 1987;
and
(d) renovation and modernization of all car control. and signalinig
equipment at the main Valle de Mexico marshaling yard; the
maiiu problems are with the newer, larger cars, in the fleet
and ex-USA, which are too long and heavy for the retarder
equipment originally installed; the new equipment, for which
preliminary studies have been done, will reduce damage and
improve the efficiency of operation.
12.       For 1986-1988, although further CTC installations are contemplated,
they are not very likely to be undertaken because of lack of funds. The
sections considered are Cordoba-Medias Aguas; Salinas Victoria-Ntuevo Laredo;
Villa de Garcia-Salinas Victoria; and Saltillo-Villa de Garcia. The last two
are presumably for the realignment under construction --Saltillo-Ramos
Arizpe-Villa de Garcia-Salinas Victoria-- and referred to in Annex 5.
G. Locomotive Recovery Program
13.       The declining state of repair of the N de M locomotive fleet has
been a source of some concern to the Bank, and discussions regarding the
preparation of an integrated locomotive recovery program were started with
the Locomotive Department of N de M over a year ago. As a result of these
discussions, a comprehensive program has been developed covering the
following aspects:
(a) a scrapping program;
(b) a program for rehabilitation of selected locomotives;
(c) purchase of new locomotives;
(d) a review of procedures and practices in workshops;
(e) a training program for selected categories of staff In
workshops;
(f) workshop extensions and improvements;
(g)  procurement of machinery 'or updating workshop operations; and
(h)  a program for procurement of locomotive spare parts.
14.       N de M's program 'or scrapping the older and generally less
reliable units in the fleet is aimed at the elimination of locomotives which
are uneconomical to repair. The total number of locomotives to be removed
during the four years 1985-1988 is 218 and will nave the effect of reducing
the number of locomotive types in the fleet by 1?, from 59 to 42. In
addition, it will remove 41 second-hand units purchased and hired in



- 85 -                               ANNEX 4
1979-1981 to help cope with a sudden upsurge of traffic at that time; this
will leave another 88 second-hand units to continue in use as long as they
can give reasonable service. A number of locomotives are out of service, -
awaiting room in the workshops or awaiting spare parts. The scrapping
program will make more room in the workshops for more useful work, while
funds being provided under the ongoing Fourth Railway Loan and the Sector
Loan will provide much of the foreign exchange required for acquiring spare
parts for repairable units.
15.       In addition, N de M has prepared a plan to rehabilitate 46 units of
the locomotive series 8290, of which 40 are in the 1985-1986 program. These
units, now 18-19 years old, have parts, diesel motors, principal generators,
control systems, etc., which are obsolete and not replaceable. Six units
have been rebuilt using new components as a trial and have been successful.
An examination of the economics of reconstructing these units, as opposed to
purchasing new units, shows that the overall life cycle cost of the
reconstructed unit is substantially lower than that of new units. It is,
thus, ensured that whatever locomotives can be repaired economically will be
rehabilitated and put back into service.
16.       An assessment of the requirements of new locomotives for the years
1984-1988 is shown in Table 4.6 of the text. In addition, N de M has made a
detailed review of locomotive needs for each type of service for the year
1985, and a similar review will be made annually thereafter. The combined
effect of improving the efficiency criteria, the integration of the
scrapping, repair and rehabilitation program, and the reduction of traffic
forecasts has been to decrease locomotive purchases from 252 in N de M's
original assessment to 100. Of these, 60 were purchased in 1984, 20 are to
be purchased in 1985, and 20 in 1986.  For 1987, the requirements of
additional motive power will be met by bringing into operation 39 electric
locomotives which have already been acquired in connection with the
electrification of the Mexico-Queretaro-Irapuato line.
17.       The overall effect of the program of scrapping, rehabilitation, new
acquisitions and other measures (detailed in para 13 preceding) results in a
recovery of the locomotive fleet by improving the locomotive availability
from the present 68% to 80% by 1988. The main feature of this program,
summarized below, is the reduction of the diesel locomotive fleet by 157
units, with substantial increases in the locomotive availability and traffic
handled:

The proposed improvement in the availability targets is incorporated into the

H. Freight Cars
18.       N de M has made a detailed assessment of its freight car needs for
1985, based upon the traffic forecast, as set out in Table 4.7 of the text.
The only shortage indicated is in covered hopper cars, and N de M proposes to
acquire 300 units. In addition, N de M proposes to purchase 160 ballast cars
for the transport of stone ballast required for the track rehabilitation and
maintenance programs. A further, more general assessment is made, in
Table 4.8 of the text, of the approximate numbers of additional cars likely
to be needed over the years 1986-1988. A total of 4,500 additional cars is
indicated, spread equally over the three years. A detailed assessment,
however, should be made by N de M each year (as was done for 1985), before
reviewing, with the Bank, the actual number of cars to be ordered in the
succeeding year.
19.       N de M is also proposing to carry out a program of rehabilitation
of freight cars, with 700 box cars to be rebuilt in 1985, half by N de M's
own workshop and half by Constructora. In the succeeding years, 1986-1988,
allowance is made for further cars to be rehabilitated.
I. Passenger Cars
20.        N de M's passenger car fleet contains a large number of aged units;
some 300, or 25%, are over 40 years old and some 600, or 50%, are over 30
years old. The Investment Program makes allowance for 30 new cars in 1985
and 100 in each year 1986-1988, or a total of 330. This is reasonable, as
long as the acquisition is limited tc replacement coaches. N de M, however,
has already contracted for a total of 100 special first-class coaches in
1985-1986, more than what is required for reF acement, and for which the
economic justification i's not sufficient. N de M is now negotiating to
revise the contract and postpone the 1986 tranche of coaches until its
economic and financial justification can be clearly shown (para 4.29).

MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
N de M Track Specifications
The N de M specifications for track rehabilitation remain generally
as in earlier projects: (a) new 115 lb/yd is normally obtained according to
American specifications, but now a proportion is obtained as higher manganese
steel, such as is commonly used on other railways, to give less wear and
longer life on sharply curved tracks (rate of wear can be 50% of that on
normal rail steel with only a relatively small increase in price); (b) the
rails are welded by the flash butt process at fixed depots into longer
lengths before laying and are then joined again in the track using the
alumino-thermit process; N de M intends to purchase a mobile flash butt
welding machine to replace the site thermit welding, in order to give a
stronger joint; (c) concrete sleepers are locally made, post-tensioned,
reinforced concrete and are placed at 1,666/km and using RS (French) elastic
clips; and (d) stone or slag ballast is provided at about 1,700 m3/km.
Recovered rail of 112 lb/yd is cropped to remove end defects and then welded
into longer lengths; sleepers used with recovered rail are generally of
impregnated timber, and fastenings are clips and screw spikes; and ballast is
provided at about 1,300 m3 per km.

MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
SCT Rail Investment Program
A. General
1.        In the 1970s, SCT developed an extensive program for construction
and modernization of railway infrastructure which was carried out as and when
funds were made available by DGVF. This program WdS reviewed during the
appraisal of the Third and the Fourth Railway Projects and, although no Bank
financing was asked for or provided, the conclusion of the reviews was that
the proposed works would help to resolve N de M's most pressing capacity and
operational problems. Many of the works were started, but progress has been
slow because of budgetary constraints. At the start of preparation of this
project, a thorough evdluation of the program was conducted in consultation
with the Bank, and a revised investment plan has been prepared which focuses
upon completing the ongoing works and, in some cases, scaling down the scope
of the works. Revised traffic forecasts and cost estimates have been applied
in the reevaluation. New priority works are proposed for construction
beyond 1985.
2.        The analysis has covered length of crossing loops, additional
crcqsing stations, strengthening of bridges to take heavier locomotives and
rolling stock, reg.-adings and realignments and relatively short stretches of
new lines to complement the realignment works. In general, N de M's works on
rehabilitation, bridge strengthening and CTC installations (Annex 4)
complement the DGVF works. The following paragraphs describe the details of
the main investment items in the 1985-1988 program.
B. Mexico-Queretaro
3.        The construction of a new double line 215 km long, between
Huehuetoca (about 30 km outside Mexico City) and Queretaro, was started 1--
the mid-1970s. It was to replace two existing single lines which carried
both through trains and local industrial traffic. The corridor carries the
heaviest traffic on the system, at about 30 million gross tons per year,
forecast by N de M to rise tc about 40 million g.-oss tons by 1990. The
original program envisaged cc..-mpletion at the end oF 1981, but budget
restrictions delayed the work, and it was about 90% complete by October 1984;
some 20 km of track remain 1o be laid, and completic.n of the work is expected
by December 1984.
4.   In 1980, the Government decided that the new lire should be electrified
and that this electrification should be carried into the Mexico City main
passenger station at Buena Vista. At the same time, it was decided to
quadruple the tracks from Huehuetoca, past the main Valle de Mexico freight
yards, into Buena Vista, a distance of 30 km, and to provide grade
separations at all the intersecting city streets, in order to enable both
fast through-passenger trains and frequent local suburban trains to operate.
This ne-w section involves major earthworks, bridging and a tunnel, and the



- 90 -                             ANNEX 5
work is in its early stages; between Huehuetoca and Lecheria (about half
way), earthworks are 5% complete and bridges 30% while, from Lecheria to
Buena Vista, the earthworks are also 5% complete, and a tunnel is 5%
complete, but all the bridging has yet to be started. Contracts for many of
the remaining works have still to be placed, and completion in all respects
is scheduled for mid-1988. The track material for the Huehuetoca to Buena
Vista section has yet to be provided.
C. Queretaro-Irapuato
5.        The continuation of double track beyond Queretaro to Irapuato, a
length of 115 km, witlh a maximum grade of 0.3 x and a minimum curve of 2°
(573 m radius), was started in late 1980, but, again, has been slow because
of budget constraints.  Earthworks are about 30% complete, and bridges 20%
complete, but track laying has not yet started. Remaining carthworks,
including those for a new marshaling yard at Irapuato, have almost all been
contracted, but 20 bridges over the whole lei,gth htave yet to be tendered.
The laying of the first 10 km of track has been tendered, but no track
material is available for the remaining length or for the Irapuato yard.
Completion is scheduled for early 1988. This new double track is also to he
electrified, buit installation will nor he started until late 1986, with
completion scheduled for 1988 or 1989.
D. Electrification, Mexico City-Queretaro-Irapuato
6.        Almost all the material and equipment for the electrifiration of
this line, including 39 electric locomotives, have been ordeied and
supplled. Site installation is now heing started after administrative
problems led to important staff changes. The new staff is reactivating the
foreign contracts and forming a site organization to deal with the
installation work, which is to start at Aragon about half way between Mexico
and Queretaro; about 40 km of installation was planned for 1984, 10() km for
1985 and 100 km for 1986, but this program seems lik-lv to be delayed. A
program for the further installation for Queretaro-Irapuato has not vet heen
defined, but is contemplated to start in late 1986 fo- completion in 1988 or
1989. The contract for the workshops may be placed in 1985, while contracts
for the signaling and telecommunications equipment still have to be
considered.
E.  Mexico-Veracruz
7.        Because of increasing traffic to and from the southern oil
development area near Coatzacoalcos, the original single "Mexicano" line
between the coastal plain and the Mexico Citv plateau, with grades up to 4%
uncompensated and curves of 12° (100 m radius), was found to have inadequate
capacity. The alternative route, via Veracruz and Jalapa, had 2.5%
compensated ruling grade and similar curvature, and its capacity was being
used fully, mainly for Veracruz traffic.  Construction of a new single line
was, therefore, started in 1978 on the Mexicano escarpment section, the new
line having a ruling grade of 2.5% compensated and minimum curvature of 6°
(200 m radius), with a length of 74 km between Los Reves and Ciudad Mendoza.
Traffic forecasts indicated that the new single line capacity might be
exceeded within 12-15 years from. completion; therefore. DGVF and N de M



- 91 -                             ANNEX 5
decided that reconstruietion of the lines toward the south and to Veracruz
shouild be in double track. Consequently, the Ciudad Mendoza-Paso del Macho
section was authorized for double-track construction. The many tunnels and
bridges already under construction from Los Reyes to Ciudad Mendoza, however,
made a late change impossible for douhling that track, so the intention, in
the ftuture, is to rehabilitate the original steep single line for use for
down-hill traffic and  i usc the new, lesser graded line for up-hill traffic.
8.         The Los Reves-Ciuidad Mendoza section is only now being completed
(some final leveling and stone hallasting remains to be done, and the
telecommtunications are still to he tendered); the section is expected to be
placed in operaLion by N de M In 1985. On the Ciudad Mendoza-Paso del Macho
section, some sections are completely new double track, while on others, a
new single line is being built parallel to the existing line.  The length is
60 km, ruling grade 1.8% and minimum curvature 6'. Earthworks are completed
or contracted, except for one 12-km section near the Paso del Macho end.
Overall, earthworks are about 50%' complete, bridges 50%, tunnels 90%. and
track work 20%. A large viaduct (Metlac) near Fortin has recently been
completed. About 60 km of track material is still needed co finish the
diouble-track length, and full completion is scheduled for mid-1987.
9.        The capacity of the new single 2.5% grade line, Los Reyes-Ciudad
Mendoza, using 64-car freighlt trains ancd 6 x 3,000 HP locomotives (as
operated on the similar grade Veracruz-Jalapa line), after installation of
CTC (as part of the N de M program for Teotihuacan-Los Reves-Cordoba-Paso del
Macho-Ver-cruz), should he about il million net tons of freight plus
pas i.ec  and local trains.  The original line capacity was about 1.5 million
net tons. The schedule should provide enough time for DGVF to deal with the
reh'abilitation of the original, steeply graded Los Reves-Ciudad Mendoza line,
as a second iine on the escarpment section. DGVF also intended to carry the
dot'lle-line construction through from Paso del Macho to Veracruz, but has now
poistponed that scheme, assuming that N de M's installation of CTC through to
Veracruz should be sufficient for some time ahead.
F. Tierra Blanca-Medias Aguas-Coatzacoalcos
10.        South of Cordoha, the existing single line to Tierra B]anca (92 km)
has a 2.5Z compensated grade, sco that 64-car freight trains should he ahle to
operate tip-hill. From Tierra Blanca south to Medias Aguas, the terrain is
generallv flatter, but the line has lengths of grade up to 2% and 2.3% in
eit;.er direction.  There are similar lengths of 2% grades between Med'as
Aguas (the junction for the line to the Pacific at Salina Cruz, also south to
Tapachula and the Guatemala border) and Coatzacoalcos. Some regrading has
already been done between Rodriguez Clara and Medias Aguas to give a ruling
grade of 1.3%; DGVF is now proposing to carry out a realignment and regrade
over some 90 km south froir Tres Valles to Azueta, making a double-traei: line,
partly by new construction and partly by laving a new single track parilllel
to the existing line.  DGVF's plan is then to zonstruct a new single line
about 150 km long, from Azueta, on a more direct route to Jaltipan, some 40
km from CoatzacoXlcos. The total present length, Tres Valles-Jaltipan, is
261 km, and the dListance by the new direct rouze would be 227 km; the new
ruling grade would be 1% and the minimum curve 10 (1,146 m radius).   After
recent reviews, DGVF has decided to proceed only with the first part of the
proposal in the period 1985-.988 and to leave the second part, the direct
line to Jaltipan, to follow in a subsequent program period.

J.  Guadalajara-Monterrey
16.       To shorten the route for mineral trains between Manzanillo and

Castillo) to Encarnacion, will avoid traffic congestion in the Irapuato area
and the second, Salinas-Laguna Seca, will connect across to the San Luis
Potosi-Saltillo main line, leading to Monterrey. The length of the first
construction is 195 km and of the second 125 ki, with ruling grades of 0.35%
and 0.20% respectively, and minimum curves of 1.50 (764 n radius). The total
length from Guadalajara to Vanegas (on the San Luis Potosi-Saltillo line)
will decrease from 724 km to 567 km. The second section, Salinas-Laguna
Seca, is the most advanced in construction, earthworks and bridging being
100% complete and trackwork 60% complete. Track material can be made
available for completion '- -id-1986. On the other section,
Guadalajara-Encarnacion, earthworks are only 10% complete and bridging 15%,
with no provision as yet for track material; final completion is not expected
until mid-1988.
K.  Monterrey-Saltillo

site; work has not yet started and is planned for 1986-1988. The second item
is the east^ru sector of the bypass, 40-km long, from the new terminal site

18.       :?rom Soledad, west of Villa de Garcia, to Ramos Arizpe, toward
Saltillo, is r climb on a ruling grade of 2%. DGVF had started work on the

give a 1% ruling grade; one contract had been let for 20 km (out of 64 km) at

proceed and whether to make the line single or double track and the ruling

N de M in the near future than the previous item, for the Soledad-Ramos

T. Veracruz-Tampicc/Mexico-Tampico
20.       These are both new lines:  the first, some 400 km long, is to
provide a new coastal route between the two cities; the second, between Honey
on the existing system on the Mexican plateau and Magosal-Tampico, some 200
km, would provide a more direct connection than the existing route via San
Luis Potosi. On the Veracruz-Tampico line, the first section of
construction, Cordel-Nautla (87 km), was started in 1980; 30 km of this
length is due for completion in mid-1985; bridges onward toward Nautla are
also under construction. Because of budget constraints, the work is being
closed down. The Mexico-Tampico direct connection is scheduled to be started
in 1987-1988. The operational and economic data are insufficient to
evaluate either of these new lines adequately, and further tenders should be
delayed until further studies are carried out.
M. Yards and Terminals
21.       With the substantial increases in traffic that have taken place in
recent years, the principal railway yards of N de M have reached the
saturation point, and delays to freight cars and trains in yards are becoming
a problem. The SCT construction program foresees five new yards at Irapuato,
Coatzacoalcos, Monterrey, Coyotopec (outside Mexico City) and Guadalajara.
To examine the problem of the yards, a technical committee was set up in 1980
comprising representatives of N de M and DGVF. This team has done some work
in analyzing the various issues involved in studying new yards and yard
equipment in the U.S. and Canada, and a certain amount of research work with
computer modeling for large yards. However, concrete progress on this front
has been limited, partly because of political and organizational problems and
partly because of lack of definition of priorities. The Coyotopec yard,
which is to relieve the major Valle de Mexico yard and on which substantial
effort has been spent, is being delayed because of land acquisition
problems. Some progress, however, has been made on the Monterrey yard, for
which land is being acquired.



- 95-                             ANNEX 5
22.       The Bank's review of the yard problem has recommended the need for
a modular approach to the development of the additional yard facilities, not
only to avoid over-ambitious planning but also to ensure that investments are
made gradually and in accordance with proven demand. Further, there is
need to develop a Master Plan for the yards on a systemwide basis that would
take into account the changing patterns of traffic. Finally, there is need
to analyze the present operations of the principal yards to ensure that
everything possible is done to improve the productivity of the existing
facilities in order to minimize investments in new facilities. As part of
the proposed project, it is expected that a Yard Master Plan Study will be
done, with the participation of foreign consultants and expertise wherever
necessary, including, inter alia, a study of yard operations to examine ways
and means of improving the efficiency of the existing installations.
23.       Meanwhile, preparatory work is to be initiated on the relatively
minor yards at Irapuato and Jaltipan (to relieve Coatzacoalcos) - the former
as it is to be the terminus of the new electrified service between Mexico and
Irapuato, and the latter because of the steep increases in the traffic caused
by the integration of the FUS and the growing demand of oil-related traffic
from the South.
