MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
Passenger Service Strategy
A.   Defining the Issue
1.        Rail passenger services in Mexico have traditionally been given
little attention since most concern of the Government and N de M has been
directed at freight services. A large number of trains were eliminated in
the mid-1970s after being declared uneconomic, and traffic declined, despite
fares set well below even short-run avoidable costs. Some equipment was
purchased in the late 1970s, but the overall fleet has deteriorated to
substandard levels. Renewed interest in passenger services has recently
arisen for several reasons:
(a) the attractiveness of rail service and traffic is apparently
growing as a result of rising fuel prices and the increasing cost
of competing services;
(b) load factors have increased to such an extent, despite highly
deteriorated equipment, that, if existing services are to be
continued, substantial investments are required;
rc) infrastructure investments on the main lines have removed capacity
restrictions, and new types of first class passenger services are
technically feasible; and
(d) the economic recession has broug'lt attention to the continuing
operating losses for passenger services.
2.        N de M prepared a paper (June 1984) which recommends a
comprehensive strategy for rail passenger services including the
identification of services to be improved, an appropriate fare policy,
required investments and a proposal for assessing the feasibility of new
services. The following is a summary of the findings and proposals of the
N de M stragegy paper. Specific recommendations and guidelines regarding the
implementation of the N de M proposals, developed during the preparation of
the sector project, are also presented. The main questions to be answered
are:
(a) which railway services are economically viable when compared with
competing transport modes;
(b) at what level should fares be set and what impact would that have
upon traffic; and
(c)  at which services should -iew investment be directed.
B. Evolution of Passenger Traffic
3.        N de M offers a number of interurban passenger services including
sleeping car service, first class special, first class and second class
services and a mixed freight and passenger train service for outlying low
density lines. Passenger traffic had declined, from over 33.2 million
passengers in 1970 to 16 million passengers in 1981, until recent increases
returned traffic levels to 22 million in 1983 (including 3 million passengers



- 97 -                           ANNEX 6
of the recently incorporated FUS line). Much of the decline was due to the
elimination of 43% of all passenger services between 1974 and 1978.
Passenger-km, however, have not declined as precipitously because the average
distance per passenger rose from about 100 km to 190 km from 1970 to 1983,
and, today, N de M carries 4.2 billion passenger-km as compared with
3.4 billion in 1970 (Table 3;9 of the text). This represents only about 1%
of all passenger-km in Mexico, with road transport being the dominant mode.
4.        About 75% of the passengers are carried in normal passenger trains
and about 25% in mixed freight/passenger trains. Furthermore, about 87% of
the passengers use the second class services while 10% use first class and
the remaining 3% use sleeping car services.
C. Level of Service
5.        The predominance of second class passengers is explained by the low
level of service that N de M is offering. On-time performance, although it
has improved recently, is still below tolerable levels for most first class
passengers, with only 21% of the main-line trains arriving on time, 48%
arriving within an hour of the scheduJa and 31% arriving between one and
three hours late. Similarly, the condition of the coaches is indicated by
the age of the fleet. Of the almost 790 coaches in the fleet (including 192
sleeping cars), about 20% are less than 10 years old and the rest are over 25
years old, with more than 200 coaches over 45 years old (Table 3.7 of the
text). Lastly, load factors on the coaches have increased to a point at
which some potential passengers are apparently not able to be served and
traffic is being turned away. The average number of passengers per coach
increased from 40 in 1977 to 63 in 1984, with certain routes at certain
periods of the year being extremely saturated.
6.        A recent survey on the trains confirmed the problems of railway
services and the types of passengers who are using the trains. About 75% of
the users stated that they used the railways because of the relatively low
fare. When asked about the types of improvements they would like to see, 32%
stated the need for more coaches, 44% wanted cleaner coaches and 31%
expressed concern over the maintenance of the stations. Interestingly, only
11% expressed concern about the on-time performance of the trains, an
indication of the low value placed on time savings by the second class
passenger.
D. Fares and Costs
7.        Fares are set below even short-run variable costs, with passenger
revenues covering about 60% of such costs. Although passenger fares had been
increased faster than inflation between 1980 and 1983, they had not been
increased at the same rate as freight and, in 1984, the advances of the
previous three years were reduced when the Government failed to raise fares
in line with inflation. The Government agreed, under the Fourth Railway
Project, to provide a specific subsidy to cover the related deficit. This
subsidy increased from about Mex$ 1.0 billion in 1980 to Mex$ 5.1 billion in
1984.  In real terms, however, the subsidy declined by 37Z over the same
period and today amounts to about US$26 million as a result of the higher
load factor per coach and per train and real tariff increases. In addition,
other factors mentioned in Annex 2, such as the overall real decline in
salaries and materials costs, affected subsidy levels.



-98 -                            ANNEX 6
8.        As N de M and the Government continue to reduce freight subsidies,
the passenger deficit represents an increasing proportion of the net
operating rail deficit, having risen from 11% in 1980 to 20% in 1984. In
addition, although passenger revenues, including Government subsidy,
represent only 7% of gross railway operating revenues, passenger services
represent 28% of all train-km.
9.        In comparison with bus transport, rail passenger fares declined
from 86% of a first class bus fare and 62% of a second class bus fare in 1970
to 59% and 38% respectively in 1984. Thus, bus fares have been increased by
the Government at a faster pace, although a general review of bus costs
indicates that bus operators are not covering the replacement cost of their
vehicles under present tariffs, requiring further real increases over the
next several years. Similarly, a comparison of the economic costs of railway
and bus services indicates that the railways maintain a substantial advantage
over buses on the high density lines, with most services averaging about
Mex$ 2-4 per passenger-km compared with about Mex$ 3-4 for bus transport.
Certain rail passenger service costs were near, or more than, those for buses
and usually related to sleeping car services, which, because of the low
number of passengers per coach (27 as compared with 63 in regular coaches),
resulted in costs of about Mex$ 5-7 per passenger-km.
E. Government and N de M Strategy and Forecast
10.       The Government and N de M have proposed a comprehensive strategy
for passenger services which includes the improvement of high density
services, the analysis of the potential for new first class services on main
lines which are being upgraded, and the gradual elimination of low density
services for which no social or economic justification can be made. The main
element in this strategy is the investment in new coaches, beginning with
replacement coaches and continuing with additional coaches for new services,
once such services are shown to be economically and financially viable. An
additional number of replacement coaches are to be acquired for the other
three railways (about 176 coaches). The initial new coaches will be assigned
to first class services.
11.       The tariff policy described in the strategy paper calls for
increasing passenger fares for first class services to cover at least
short-run variable costs in the near future and progressively increasing
thereafter to cover long-run variable costs. Similarly with sleeping car
services, the paper proposes the same tariff policy, with the understanding
that this would divert passengers to other rail or bus services and lead to a
decline in sleeping car services. For second class services, the paper
proposes gradual increases to short-run variable costs, with compensation for
the deficit to be provided by the Government to N de M and classified in a
manner equivalent to that used for fare revenues in the N de M balance
sheets. Any new services would be expected to cover the full costs of those
services.
12.       Based on this strategy, N de M has forecast that total passenger
trips (excluding the introduction of new services) will grow from 22.5
million in 1984 to 24.8 million in 1988 and passenger-km from 4.3 billion in
1984 to 5.0 billion in 1988, for an average annual growth of 2.5% and 3.8%



- 99 -                           ANNEX 6
respectively (Table 3.9 of the text).  The forecast assumes that all growth
will occur on the high density lines while the lower density lines' traffic
will either remain stable or decline.
F. Recommendations
13.       While the strategy is relatively sound, questions still arise over
how it will be implemented and what specific criteria are to be applied in
setting fares, projecting demand and assessing investments. Some initial
actions, i.e., with regard to investments, appear not to be consistent with
the proposed strategy. The following recommendations address these questions
and provide a basis for formulating a plan of action under the Railway Sector
Project.
14.       Passenger Fares.  The Government has indicated that, with regard to
passenger fares, it is not considered feasible to raise fares substantially,
in real terms, until the proposed improvements are made because of the
divergence between appropriate long-run fares and the present level of
service. It is recommended that:
(a) a detailed costing of mainline passenger services be carried out by
N de M, distinguishing between types of services, by November 1986;
(b) passenger fares be increased gradually between 1985 and 1988,
reaching the level of long-run variable costs by January 1988; and
'c) a Government-specific subsidy for losses incurred for passenger
serv'ces be provided to N de M in a timely manner each year.
15.       Demand Forecast.  The demand forecast for each passenger line and
type of service should be based on the fare policy to be established and the
comparative levels of service and fares on competing modes. Appropriate
surveys should be conducted to help estimate the cross-elasticities between
modes.
16.       Investment.  An initial evaluation of the acquisition of passenger
coaches indicated a serious need and acceptable rate of return for
replacement coaches, but did not support the railways' recent contracting for
100 first-class special coaches to be delivered in 1985 and 1986. Traffic is
insufficient, and there is a more substantial need for first and second-class
replacement coaches. N de M is reviewing its initial acquisition of
first-class special coaches in order to limit the order to those already set
for delivery in 1985. Further purchases of first-class special coaches
should await appropriate marketing studies which take into account the
effects of the proposed tariff policy on demand forecasts and confirm the
economic and financial feasibility of such coaches.
April 1985



-100 -                            ANNEX 7
MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
Economic Evaluation Guidelines for the N de M Investment Plan
and Results of 1985 Evaluation
A. General Methodology
1.        The approach of the Sector Project for the economic evaluation of
N de M's investments is to establish a set of guidelines to be applied
annually by N de M during its normal budgetary preparations covering all
investment commitments to be made in the following year.  For the purpose of
the appraisal of the Sector Project, the guidelines were applied to
investment commitments to be made in 1985.  The following summarizes the
agreed upon guidelines and the results of the economic evaluation of the 1985
investments. The discussion covers the following major types of investments:
(a) acquisition and rehabilitation of motive power;
(b) acquisition and rehabilition of freight rolling stock;
Cc) acquisition of passenger coaches;
Cd) track rehabilitation with new rail;
Ce) track rehabilitation with recovered rail; and
Cf) signaling.
A technical evaluation is to be made of other rail investments such as
telecommunications and workshop machinery.
2.        The N de M Investment Plan is an integrated package with a number
of interrelated components. For example, the realization of benefits from
capacity-increasing investments, such as investments in rolling stock and
motive power, is dependent upon the availability of adequately maintained
infrastructure which will allow commercial speeds, and locomotive and car
utilization envisaged under the Plan of Action. In turn, the benefits from
works such as track renewal, which prevent line deterioration, thereby
avoiding capacity restrictions, are dependent, in part, upon the availability
of adequate motive power and rolling stock to carry forecast traffic. In the
evaluation of the N de M Investment Plan, each of the investments is
evaluated separately to determine its economic viability. Because of the
interrelationship between the subprojects, however, some degree of overlap is
unavoidable in evaluating each separately. Thus, for example, it is assumed,
when evaluating infrastructure investments (track renewal and signaling),
that expansions of fleet and motive power will take place to sustain the
increasing traffic. Their depreciation is included in the operating costs on
these lines, and it is assumed that the operational improvements under the
Action Plan will be realized.
B. Acquisition and Rehabilitation of Motive Power and Rolling Stock
3.        In 1985, N de M expects to acquire 20 new locomotives and to
rehabilitate 20 locomotives, as well as to purchase 460 new wagons (including
160 ballast wagons) and to rehabilitate 700 wagons. The economic evaluation
of investments in acquisition and rehabilitation of motive power and rolling
stock assumes a without-project case in which investments are not made



-101-                             ANNEX 7
(including investments in renewal motive power, which has reached the end of
its economic life), and, consequently, traffic is diverted to road at a
higher economic cost. The commodities which would most likely be diverted
are identified, and the economic cost differential between road and rail for
those commodities is calculated.
4.        Project benefits consist of the net savings derived from rail
transportation as compared with road transport for the door-to-door movement
of goods. Truck operating costs are calculated for a truck (5-axle
tractor-semi trailer) with an average load of 12 tons (including backhauls);
economic vehicle operating costs are based on estimates used for road
investment evaluations by SCT. Truck costs, which include the estimated
contribution of the diverted traffic to the marginal cost of road
maintenance, were taken at Mex$ 3.94 per ton-km 11. Long-run variable rail
costs, excluding the capital cost of the wagons and locomotives (since these
are included in the cost stream for the economic return calculation) but
including terminal costs, averaged Mex$ 1.20 per ton-km. In both cases,
diesel fuel was priced at an opportunity cost of US$0.85 per gallon. A
shorter useful life is assumed for rehabilitated equipment (12 years compared
with 20 years for new locomotives, and 18 years compared with 30 years for
new wagons).
5.        It was estimated that, without the 40 locomotives and 460 wagons,
the potential traffic diversion could reach 1.9 billion ton-km. The economic
analysis evaluated the investment in rolling stock and motive power together
and separately. Results of these evaluations are presented in Table I of
this annex and show an estimated rate of return of 21X for the combined
locomotive and wagon investment, along with workshop equipment, 74% for
locomotives alone and 33% for wagons alone.
6.        In deciding whether to purchase new locomotives or to rehahilitate
a number of existing locomotives in appropriate condition, a life-cycle cost
comparison was made which assumed that, while the initial rehabilitation cost
would he lower than purchasing a new locomotive, the maintenance costs would
be higher, and the useful life would be shorter (12 years versus 20 years for
a new locomotive). In addition, it is probable that the operating cost of
the rehabilitated locomotive would be greater in terms of fuel consumption,
and, for the purposes of this analysis, it is assumed that the rehabilitated
locomotive would consume about 30% more fuel than a new locomotive. The
life-cycle cost differential is substantial in favor of rehabilitation, with
the total discounted costs of the rehabilitated locomotive representing 68%
of the new one (Table 2 of this annex).
C. Track Renewal Program
7.        In 1985, N de M is expected to carry out a program of selective
track renewal on 339 km of its routes with traffic ranging from 2.8 to 4.9
million net tons, using 115 lb/yd rail with elastic type fastenings which are
supported on concrete sleepers.  Also, the program considers the procurement
of machinery to support the renewal works, as well as maintenance operations.
8.        The quantified benefits from main line track renewal are (a) the
reduction in track maintenance costs, including avoidance of investment in
deferred maintenance to bring the line- zo a minimum standard of safety; and
l/   In end-1983 Mex$ with an exchange rate of US$1.00: MexS 150.



-102 -                            ANNEX 7
(b) avoidance of track deterioration which, without the project and in spite
of routine and deferred maintenance, would lead to extensive speed
restrictions, ultimately limiting line capacity and causing diversion of
traffic from rail to road. Benefits from reduced wear and, therefore,
maintenance of locomotives and rolling stock, and from increased safety, were
not quantified. Table 3 of this annex give details of the economic
evaluation by line. Maintenance cost savings calculations were based on the
following assumptions. In the "without" case, first year maintenance costs
represent the cost of deferred maintenance needed to upgrade the line to a
minimum level of safety. In subsequent years, maintenance costs are based on
the cost of maintaining the existing 100 lb/yd rails, calculated at
Mex$ 992,000 per km, and increasing proportionately with traffic. In the
"with" project case, maintenance costs are based on the cost of maintaining
115 lb/yd rail. These costs increase from about Mex$ 100,000 to Mex$ 900,000
per km (depending on the line) by year 10, increasing at the same rate as the
traffic thereafter. In calculating benefits from avoiding diversion of
traffic from rail to road transport, a line capacity analysis was carried out
which identified the year in which speed reductions from rail deterioration,
given increasing traffic, would result in an intolerable level of service.
To arrive at ton-km diverted from rail to road, it was assumed that, in the
-'-without" project case, the train velocity in the limiting section would
decrease by 5 km/hr per year, until a limiting level of service was reached,
and any future increase in traffic would be diverted. Tons diverted were
multiplied by the average rail haul. Since sections to bw tm-proved belong to
different corridors, no double counting occurs.  To quantify the benefit from
avoidance of traffic diversion, the difference between long-run variable rail
and truck costs per ton-km (including terminal costs) was calculated.   These
costs include depreciation of wagons and motive power, thus assuming that
rolling stock and locomotives adequate to meet traffic increases would be
available. Investment in these items is, thus, implicitly evaluated in the
analysis.
9.        Track renewal with recovered rail is to be carried out on 260 km of
secondary lines (traffic ranging from 0.7 to 1.9 million gross tons).
Benefits from track renewal with recovered rail include (a) reduced track
maintenance costs and (b) improved equipment utilization due to increased
speeds on lines which, without the project, would be subject to speed
restrictions. Maintenance costs without the project were based on the
maintenance of existing 85 lb/yd rails "emplanchuelado" and supported by wood
sleepers; they were calculated at about Mex$ 1,000,000 per km and increasing
at the same rate as traffic. In the "with" project case, maintenance costs
were assumed to rise from Mex$ 100,000/km in year 2 to Mex$ 943,000/km by
year 10, increasing at the same rate as traffic thereafter. To quantify
savings from removal of speed restrictions, a typical train comprising 2.3
locomotives of 3,000 HP with 40 wagons and 2,122 tons was considered.
Quantified savings vary from Mex$ 0.0477 to 0.115 per gross-ton/km depending
upon the scale of speed improvements. The results of the benefit-cost
analysis are shown in Table 3 of this annex.
10.       The results of the evaluation showed estimated economic rates of
return from 18% to 67% for rehabilitation with new rail, and from 31% to 36%
for rehabilitation works with recovered rail. The estimated rate of return
for the total rehabilitation program, including track machinery for
maintenance and rehabilitation force account works, is 19%.



- 103 -                           ANNEX 7
D.   Acquisition and Installation of Systems/or Centralized Traffic
Control (CTC)
11.       In 1985, N de M is expecting to complete CTC installation for
Benjamin Mendez-San Luis Potosi.   This investment was analyzed under Loan
1929-ME with an estimated rate of return of 40% and is virtually complete.
In addition, N de M expects to initiate the acquisition of CTC equipment for
installation on 404 km of the Mexico-Veracruz line in an effort to postpone
costly double-tracking on the remaining single-track sections of the route.
The average traffic on this line is about 16-18 trains per day. This
investment was also analyzed under Loan 1929-ME (estimated rate of return of
33%), but was not executed, and it has been reassessed, applying the
procedures discussed in the following paragraph.
12.       Investments in CTC are evaluated on the basis of savings in train
operating costs derived from the improvement in the traffic flow where the
need to wait for instructions during train meets or passings is eliminated.
Based on empirical data, N de M considers, for the -with' and "without"
project cases, the transit and waiting time for the type of train formation
that each case can accommodate. Taking into account the consumption of fuel
and oil, the amortization of tractive power and rolling stock and crew costs,
the trains transit and waiting times are translated into costs with and
without CTC. The combined estimated economic rate of return for three route
sections, based on the flow of benefits and costs in Table 4 of this annex,
is 34%.
E. Acquisition of Inter-City Passenger Coaches
13.       N de M has already contracted with the local car manufacturing
company to provide, in 1985 and 1986, 100 first-class special passenger
coaches. The economic evaluation of investments in passenger coaches is
mostly based on the benefits derived from avoiding traffic diversion to more
costly transport modes that would occur if the present N de M condemnation
program of obsolete coaches takes place without replacement. Investment in
coaches to provide new first-class services will require a full marketing
analysis, and the decision criteria will be based on the ability of the
serv,ce to achieve full cost recovery, including a return on capital.
14.       Underlying the measure of the economic merits of the investments in
passenger coaches is a detailed strategy of allocating existing and/or new
coaches to specific lines and type of services in order to maximize the
returns from the investment. In the "without" project case, the equipment
remaining after the condemnation program takes effect is assigned to the
lines with lowest operating cost per passenger-km so that traffic diversion
occurs on the lines with the highest operating costs. For the "with" project
case, new coaches are assigned to a specific service on the line with the
lowest operating costs, and the coaches currently used are transferred to the
best alternative use on the same line or on a line with higher operating
costs, resulting in a cascading of equipment. The cost-benefit of avoiding
diversion on another line is then evaluated. If investments on a specific
line and service are found to be justified, the economic analysis continues
for investments on the next line in terms of operating costs. This iterative
process stops when the optimum number of passenger coaches to be acquired is
found.



- 104 -                           ANNEX 7
15.       An initial analysis of the investment in coaches showed that
N de M's decision to acquire 100 first-class special coaches in 1985-lqR6 was
not sufficiently justified and that, at least, the 1986 tranche shonld he
postponed, if contractually feasible, and more attention plAced on first and
second-class replacement coaches. More detailed financial and economic
evaluations are required, particularly for the first-classR coaches. The
forecast of demand for passenger services on lines to which the new coaches
would be assigned should take into account the impact of the planned increase
in passenger fares, as well as the improved quality of services (Annex 6).
F. Other Types of Investment
16.       There are other N de M investments for which an economic evaluation
was not considered feasible, and only a technical evaluation was made to
confirm the utility of the proposed items. The major component in this
category is telecommunications, including the extension of communication
lines and equipment to stations and yards, and the equipping of locomotives
with radios, following the proposals of the consultant study financed under
the Second Railway Project (Loan 825-ME). The telecommunications investment
for 1985 totals Mex$ 3,244 million (US$15.1 million). N de M is carrying out
a post-evaluation of the benefits of prior telecommunications investments and
will use the results in assessing future investments.
17.       Also, workshop and track machinery have not received separate
economic evaluations, but have been reviewed from a technical perspective
(Annex 4). The costs of these items have been includeA in the evaluation of
locomotives and rolling stock for workshop machinerv, and in the evaluation
of rail rehabilitation for track machinery.
G. Evaluation Criteria
18.       All evaluated investments, whether subject to Bank financing nr
not, must have a minimum estimated economic rate of return of 12X unless
otherwise agreed with the Bank. Appropriate sensitivity tests are to he
applied to factors in the analysis subject to a relative degree of
uncertainty, including traffic and costs.
19.       In developing appropriate sensitivity tests, the foctis has heen on
the scope and timing of the diversion of traffic, the basis for much of the
calculated benefits. In some cases, equipment may he used a few more years
with higher maintenance costs, or traffic may take more time to respond to
declines in speeds related to a lack of rehabilitation or inadequate
signaling. The specific costs are difficult to quantify and, thus, a
sensitivity check of lagging traffic diversion, over a period of five years,
has been included in the locomotive and wagon analysis. Further refinements
to this analysis will be developed during the project and applied In the
annual investment review.
March 1985

-109-                             ANNEX 8
MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
Economic Evaluation Guidelines for the SCT Investment Program
and Results of 1985 Evaluation
A. General Methodology
1.        As with the N de M investment plan, the approach of the Sector
Project for the economic evaluation of the SCT investments is to establish a
set of guidelines to be applied annually by SCT during its normal budgetary
preparations, covering all investment commitments to he made in the following
year. For the purpose of the appraisal of the Sector Project, the guidelines
were applied to subprojects which are to be committed in 1985, or for which
substantial expenditures are still required in 1985 to continue and/or
complete the works. The following summarizes the agreed upon guidelines and
the results of the economic evaluation of the 1985 investments.
2.        The economic evaluation of the SCT program for track realignment,
double-tracking, bypasses and new lines is based upon benefits derived from
the elimination of capacity and/or major operational constraints. The
methodology used for the evaluation of the subprojects included in the
program quantified the following benefits:
(a) operating cost savings due to the reduction of controlling ruling
grades and curvatures;
(b) savings derived from increased capacity, calculated as savings in
transport costs due to the avoidance of traffic diversion from rail
to road, or from rail to more costly rail lines;
(c) savings in time and vehicle operating costs accruing to road users
because of bypass rail lines to avoid urban developments; and
(d) reduced investments in new locomotives due to the release of
helpers" when grades are reduced.
B. Savings in Train Operating Costs
3.        Operating cost savings due to the reduction of controlling ruling
grades and curvatures are quantified as the difference between operating
costs in the "with' and 'without' project cases. The methodology basically
consists of the determination of the cost of operating a typical train in the
different sections of the line under study, as a function of the motive power
necessary to overcome the limiting sections.



-110-                             ANNEX 8
4.        Train operating costs are based mainly upon the quantification of
the following elements:
(a) amortization costs of the motive power needed for an adequate
operation of a typical train on the line, taking into account the
normal scheduled time as a function of the projected traffic;
(b) amortization costs of wagons;
(c) cost of fuel and oil consumed; and
(d) cost of the train crew.
C. Capacity
(i) Description of the Methodology Used for Evaluating Capacity
5.        To assess when individual lines would reach their capacity limits
and further demand would have to be diverted to more costly alternatives,
N de 1x and SCT have carried out a detailed series of analyses. Simple
theoretical assessments are made manually of the limiting number of trains
under existing conditions and with various improvements, such as increasing
the number of crossing stations, increasing the length of crossing stations
to take longer, heavier freight trains, introducing centralized traffic
control (CTC), relocation to reduce ruling grade and curvature, and provision
of double-track on controlling sections. These assessments are checked with
train diagrams and train timings. Traffic forecasts are developed to help
determine the appropriate timing for alternative additional capacities.
Costs and benefits are then assessed for the alternative or staged
investments.
(ii) Adequacy of the Caacity Analysis and Present Action Plan
6.        The methodology described above is adequate for identifying
potential bottlenecks and the years in which they are likely to occur, but it
will be necessary for SCT and N de M to develop more sophisticated and
detailed techniques during the course of the project for analyzing the
cost-effectiveness of alternative solutions for expanding capacity and/or
improving operating performance with regard to new potential investments. To
this end, it is proposed that the railways acquire and introduce a type of
computer program used by most other railways in developed countries called a
Train Performance Calculator (TPC). This type of computer program can be
used not only in performing capacity analysis, but also in many other areas,
such as:
(a) evaluation of fuel usage under varying operating conditions;
(b) scheduling of trains (most railroads now use TPC programs for
running time information for their train preparation);
(c) evaluation of time savings through various operating changes, such
as different powering strategies; and
(d) better information for use in any economic evaluation, such as
improving the quality of present calculations for fuel consumption.



-111 -                            ANNEX 8
7.        The TPC program requires details of the track profile as an input,
which do not appear to be readily available. The first step, then, for the
i.mplementation of the program would be to carry out line surveys.
Implementation of a TPC program, as an established day-to-day tool of both
Planning and Operations, is expected to take about two years. After that
period, the railway would have the necessary capability to consider the
acquisition, or creation, of even more detailed capacity analysis tools. The
Introduction of a TPC program, however, should be considered a first step
toward the ultimate objective of evaluating capacity through the use of a
redispatch technique - to stage the expansion plans for N de M's main lines.
D. Quantification of Benefits Accruing to Road Users Because of Relocation
of Lines
8.        For those projects in which rail lines are relocated to bypass
urban developments, SCT quantified the benefits derived from savings in
vehicle operating costs and passenger time accruing to road users because of
the elimination of level rail crossings. To that end, SCT considered two
'without" project situations:
(a)  that in which a train Is approaching the road and vehicles are
forced to stop and wait for the train to pass; and
(b)  that in which vehicles reduce their velocity only to effect the
rail crossing.
9.        The type and number of vehicles affected by each of the situations
are derived from the application of a Poisson probability distribution to
traffic data compiled for SCT's 1983 study, "The Specific Characteristics of
Road Users and Their Vehicles on the Basic Road Network." The study also
determined vehicle occupancy and the value of the time of drivers and
passengers for those vehicles considered in the analysis.
10.       To calculate vehicle operating costs in the "without" project
case, it was assumed that a vehicle approaching a rail crossing for which
signals are warning of a coming train, the process of deceleration and
acceleration to normal velocity after waiting for the train to pass takes
place over a road length of 100 m. The actual waiting time is derived as a
function of the velocity and the length of a train, which SCT estimates for
each line and for each type of train (direct, unit trains or passengers).
The -with' project case assumes that vehicles will reduce their velocities
slightly to effect a bridge-crossing.
E.   Reduced Investments in New Locomotives
11.       In those cases in which, because of reduced grades, "helper"
locomotives are no longer needed and can be incorporated into the rest of the
system, a one-year benefit, equal to the price of a new locomotive times the
number of locomotives released, is considered. The underlying assumption is
that the railway can reduce the number of locomotives in its purchase program
in the year subsequent to the completion of the project.



-112 -                            ANNEX 8
F.  Yard Investments
12.       Substantial investments are being considered to relocate major rail
yards in Mexico City, Monterrey, Guadalajara and Coatzacoalcos. Neither
N de M nor SCT have sufficient technical tools to assess these investments,
and, under the project, they should carry out a master plan of yard needs for
the network, focusing upon the projected future workload of yard facilities.
This plan would include an operational study to explore the possibility of
better handling efficiency in existing terminals. To conduct this study,
consultant assistance would be required, especially to install appropriate
computer models and to train local staff in the use of computer models in
analyzing yard capacity and design. The economic evaluation would be a
component of the master plan study.
G. Electrification Works
13.       No detailed analysis of the electrification works between
Mexico-Queretaro-Irapuato was conducted because of the level of completion of
those works. The equipment and materials have already been purchased
(including locomotives and catenaries), and the remaining investment relates
to final installation. A general evaluation, however, was carried out to
determine if the level of traffic was sufficient to justify electrification
following the methodology described in the Bank's Staff Working Paper
No. 634. 1/ In accordance with this methodology, it was found that the
traffic of 46.5 million gross tons on the Mexico-Queretaro line and 28.5
million gross tons between Queretaro and Irapuato exceeded the minimum
required.   No further electrification works are contemplated for the period.
H. Economic Criteria and Evaluation Results for 1985 Program
14.       All subprojects in the SCT rail investment program should have an
estimated economic rate of return of at least 12%. Sensitivity analyses
should consider a shortfall in projected traffic of 15% and a delay in
expected timing of traffic diversion. The results of the evaluation of the
1985 investment program are summarized in Table 1 of this annex along with
the characteristics of each subproject. Since many of the subprojects are
ongoing, the analysis was carried out with and without sunk costs. The
results were:
(a) that the program is economically viable with the exception of the
double-tracking between Ramos Arizpe and Villa Garcia. Excluding
this section, the estimated economic rates of return were above
15Z;
(b) that the ongoing work on one subsection of the Ramos Arizpe and
Villa Garcia line should be finished and put into operation, but
that the remaining subsections should not be tendered unless
further analysis of those subsections or a revised program for them
indicates the economic viability of the works; and
1/  L. Alston, -Railways and Energy," World Bank Staff Working Papers,
Number 634, 1984.



-113 -                            ANNEX 8
(c)  that the investments in Veracruz-Tampico and Hexico-Tampico
required more detailed evaluations, in terms of traffic potential
and the comparative advantage of rail over other modes, and should
be postponed.
15.       It was also found, in the evaluation of ongoing subprojects, that
alternative levels of improvement were not always compared sufficiently.
More appropriate alternatives analysis is to be performed for any new
investments in the SCT program, and more sophisticated modeling capabilities
will need to be provided under the project. Subprojects to be included in
the investment program in subsequent years will, thus, be analyzed by
applying more refined procedures.
M-arch 1985

- 115 -                           ANNEX 9
MEXICO
RAILWAY SECTOR PROJECT
Project Implementation
A. Annual Updating of Investment Programs
1.        By November of each year during project execution, beginning in
1985, N de M and SCT would update their rolling four-year rail investment
programs, applying procedures and criteria agreeable to the Bank (para 4.05
of the report). The updating would consist of revised traffic forecasts, the
technical and economic evaluation of new investment commitments to be made
under the following year's budget, the review of costs for ongoing
investments and the reevaluation of those investments whose real costs are
expected to increase by more than 20%, and a projection of investment needs
in the subsequent years, taking into consideration probable budgetary
levels. The Bank would carry out a review of the updated proDgrams in time
for SCT and N de M to incorporate Bank comments in the finalized budget. SCT
and N de M would provide, annually, a listing of the items to be financed
under the loan in the subsequent year and any related data that the Bank
shall reasonably request.
B. Procurement
2.        The principal items to be purchased with the proposed Bank loan
would be rails, track and workshop machinery and components for freight cars
and passenger coaches. In addition, the estimated foreign exchange content
of selected SCT civil works in the project would be financed from the
proposed loan. All items to be procured using the proceeds of the proposed
loans, as well as the civil works contracts, would be subject to
international competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance with Bank guidelines.
Also, financing for consultant services and for the foreign exchange content
of the training program would be provided under the project following Bank
guidelines for procurement.
3.        The tenders for rails, machinery and parts for N de M would be
coordinated by N de M (Sub-Gerencia de Adquisiciones) whereas those required
by SCT would be processed by the Direccion General de Vias Ferreas (DGVF)
of SCT. In bid evaluation, Mexican manufacturers would be allowed a
preferential margin of 15% of the CIF cost of -.ompeting imports, or the
relevant prevailing level of custom duties, whichever is lower. Tenders In
connection with civil works for SCT would be processed by DGVF after
satisfactory review by the Bank of the technical and economic evaluations of
the candidate investments provided by SCT or its consultants. For goods
having an estimated contract value equal to or less than US$500,000
equivalent, tender documents and proposed awards would not be subject to
prior review provided that the aggregate of such contract amounts does not
exceed US$50 million equivalent. For goods having an estimated contract
value of more than US$500,000 equivalent and all remaining goods contracts,
all draft tender documents and proposed awards would be subject to prior
review. All civil works draft tender documents and procedures would be
subject to prior review. Proposed awards, however, would not be subject to



- 116 -                           ANNEX 9
prior review. The time allowed for preparation of civil works bids would be
45 days for tenders having an estimated contract amount of less than US$10
million equivalent, 60 days for those with estimated contract amounts of more
than US$10 million equivalent but less than US$20 million equivalent, and 90
days for larger contracts. For preparation of bids for goods, the time
allowed would be 45 days for tenders having an estimated contract amount of
less than US$5 million equivalent, 60 days for those with estimated contract
amounts of more than US$5 million equivalent but less than US$20 million
equivalent, and 90 days for larger contracts.
4.        The components for freight cars and passenger coaches, to be
procured through ICB and financed under the loan, would be used in the
manufacture of freight cars and passenger coaches by Constructora Nacional de
Carros (CN), a state-owned enterprise which operates efficiently on a
commercial basis and produces good quality cars at internationally
competitive prices; these prices are subject to Government review and
approval. CN's factories have the capability to produce 3,000-3,500 freight
cars and up to 200 passenger coaches per year. Therefore, the Mexican
Government and N de M have requested, in keeping with the Government's policy
of utilizing installed capacity, that construction and final assembly of
freight cars and passenger coaches be reserved for CN during the prciect
period and that Bank financing be provided only for directly imported
components, as in the Fourth Railway Project, estimated at about 10-15% of
the final car and coach price.
5.        The design and specifications of the freight cars to be
manufactured by CN are considered satisfactory. However, the proposed design
for the passenger coaches is relatively old in terms of current practice in
the rest of the world, where tubular, all-welded, anti-telescopic designs are
being used which are not only lighter, but also relatively safer, in case of
acc4dents. The Bank has, therefore, recommended to N de M and CN that, with
the substantial number of passenger coaches to be manufactured in the next
few years, CN should explore the possibility of modernizing the design of the
coach and importing the required technology to manufacture the new design.
If, therefore, this change of design can be accomplished without an
uneconomic increase in the price of the coach due to the change in
technology, it would be desirable to adopt the revised designs.
C. Disbursements
6.        For imported goods, disbursements would be made on the basis of CIF
costs, and, with respect to bids won by local manufacturers under ICB,
disbursements would be made on the basis of 100% of the ex-factory costs.
With regard to civil works, the Bank would finance 100% of the estimated
direct and indirect foreign exchange costs, amounting to 35% of the total
cost of each work (excluding value added taxes, IVA) or 100% of direct
foreign exchange expenditures. The Bank would also finance 100% of the cost
of consultant services, which is equivalent to the foreign exchange cost, and
100% of the foreign exchange costs for equipment, computer software and
scholarships under the technical assistance and training programs. A special
account would be established following procedures agreed to under ongoing
Bank operations in Mexico. The estimated disbursement schedule for the
proposed loan, based upon the assumption that the loan would become effective
by November 1985, is shown in Table 4.13 of the report. The closing date
would be June 30, 1992.



-117 -                            ANNEX 9
D.   Accounting and Auditing
(i) N de H
7.        N de M has a competent accounting staff, and the accounts are
well managed and reasonably up-to-date. Much of the accounting has,
until recently, been processed manually, but, in May 1984, the Accounts
Department installed its own Burroughs B-21 Computer. Operating expenses
will be processed first, followed by operating revenues; this should help
to speed up production of periodic financial data. Technical assistance
should be provided under the project to help N de H to improve its
accounting function, particularly in computer operations and in providing
more detailed information for traffic costing.
8.        Internal audit is carried out by a unit operating independently of
the finance department, and reporting directly to the Director General.
The Government Secretariat of Control ("Contraloria") does not audit N de X's
accounts and relies upon the external auditors. However, the appointment of
auditors requires its approval, and their report is reviewed by
Contraloria before being issued.
9.        External audit is performed annually by independent professional
auditors, and it should be determined that N de M would continue to have
external audits carried out annually, in accordance with sound auditing
principles, by an independent auditor, and would forward the audit reports
and management letters to the Bank not later than six months after the close
of each fiscal year. A new auditing firm, acceptable to the Bank, was
selected by N de M and the Contraloria as of 1984.
(ii) DGVF
10.       Unless other auditing procedures are agreed upon with the Mexican
Government, proper use of loan proceeds would be controlled by the General
Directorate of Control of SCT, which is itself controlled "externally' by
the Secretaria de la Contraloria General de la Federacion, as well as being
subject to control by SPP. The latter arrangement is that which has
provisionally served the Bank's auditing requirements with regard to
Government ministries.
E. Project Monitoring
11.       SCT and N de M confirmed that they would prepare periodic reports
for the Bank in accordance with an agreed schedule and format covering the
progress of the project, and that they would prepare a project completion
report within six months of the closing date. In addition, the Bank would
meet semi-annually with Government and railway officials to discuss project
execution.
April 1985
